The Error of Making a Concomitant a Genus
Author refutes defining 'absence of death' as 'eternal life' by showing it makes a concomitant the genus of an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a conceptual attribute is mistakenly used as a genus for an accidental property and explain why it is invalid.

Core Idea
A genus must be one in essence across species; a concomitant cannot serve as a genus.

In this passage, the author warns against a common mistake in definition: taking a necessary concomitant (like eternal life) and treating it as the genus for an accidental property (like absence of death). We will analyze why this fails and what conditions a true genus must satisfy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the flawed definition
Find in the Arabic the claim that 'absence of death is eternal life'. Note that the author uses this as an example of a conceptual attribute being made a genus for a non-constitutive affection.
2
Determine the status of 'eternal life'
The author says eternal life is a thing that is followed and necessitated by absence of death. This means it is a concomitant (lāzim), not a genus. Why? Because the genus must be predicated essentially, but here absence of death is accidental to life.
A concomitant is something that necessarily accompanies but does not enter the essence.
3
Apply the genus condition
A true genus must be one in nature and apply equally to all its species. If eternal life were the genus for absence of death, it would have to apply to both mortal and immortal things, which differ in essence. This violates the unity of the genus.
The passage explicitly states that the nature of the genus is one and the same in number.
4
Test with the example of a single life
The author notes that a single life may acquire the property 'absence of death' after not having it. This shows that absence of death is accidental, not essential. A genus cannot be acquired accidentally.
If it were a genus, it would be inseparable from the species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المواضع أيضًا ما إذا جعل المتعقل جنسًا للانفعال الغير المقوم كمن يقول إن عدم الموت حياة أزلية فإن الحياة الأزلية أمر يتبعه ويلزمه ويعرض له عدم الموت. وإنه لا يكون بطريق الحق فإن الحياة الواحدة قد كانت موصوفة بعدم الموت بعد ما لم تكن موصوفة بذلك، والحياة الأزلية إن كانت جنسا لعدم الموت والحياة مطلقاً جنس له فيكون قد صار الشيء عادمًا للموت بعد ما لم يكن؛ وطبيعة الجنس واحدة بعينها بالعدد فلا يمكن أن يوجد في الأمرين المختلفين ذاتا أعني المائت وغير المائت.
And among the topics also is when one makes the conceptual genus for a non-constitutive affection, like someone saying that absence of death is eternal life. For eternal life is a thing that is followed, necessitated, and accidentally attached to it is absence of death. And it is not in the right way; for a single life may have been described as absent of death after it was not described as such. And if eternal life is a genus for absence of death, and life absolutely is a genus for it, then a thing would become devoid of death after it was not. And the nature of the genus is one and the same in number, so it cannot exist in two things differing in essence, namely the mortal and the non-mortal.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage warns against making a conceptual concomitant (like eternal life) the genus for an accidental property (like absence of death). The example: someone says 'absence of death is eternal life'. But eternal life is a necessary concomitant of life itself; it is not a genus. Moreover, if eternal life were the genus for absence of death, then the genus would have to be one and the same in number across all its species, but it would then apply to both mortal and immortal things, which differ in essence. This is impossible because a genus is predicated uniformly of its species. Additionally, a single life can acquire the property of being death-free after not having it, which shows that absence of death is accidental and not essential to life. Hence, such a definition is invalid.

Key Terms
المتعقل
انفعال غير مقوم
عدم الموت
حياة أزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is defining 'absence of death' as 'eternal life' logically invalid?
A) Eternal life is an accident, not a genus
B) Absence of death cannot be defined by another property
C) The genus would have to apply to both mortal and immortal things, which differ in essence
D) Eternal life is a species, not a genus

Key Takeaway
A genus must be predicated uniformly across all its species; a concomitant cannot serve as a genus because it does not meet the condition of essential predication and numeric unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Unity of Genus Across Species
Author refutes defining 'absence of death' as 'eternal life' by showing it makes a concomitant the genus of an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that a genus must be one in nature and predicated equally across all its species to evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A genus cannot apply to two essences that differ in kind, such as mortal and immortal.

A key principle in definition is that a genus must be one in number (bi-aynihā bi-l-'adad) – it must have a single nature that applies to all its species without variation. The author uses this principle to refute the definition of absence of death as eternal life.

Guided Path
1
Extract the principle of genus unity
Find in the Arabic the phrase 'طبيعة الجنس واحدة بعينها بالعدد' (the nature of the genus is one and the same in number). This is a fundamental rule: a genus cannot differ across its species.
This means that the essence of the genus is identical in each species; it does not admit of degrees or variation.
2
Identify the two contrary essences
The author mentions 'the mortal' (al-mā'it) and 'the non-mortal' (ghayr al-mā'it). These are two different essences. If eternal life were the genus for absence of death, it would have to be present in both, but that is impossible because one lacks mortality and the other does not.
Contrast with a real genus like 'animal' which applies equally to both mortal and immortal beings if they are animals – but here the issue is that the genus itself (eternal life) implies immortality, so it cannot be a genus for a property that can exist in mortals.
3
Apply the principle to a new example
Consider the definition 'man is a speaking animal'. The genus 'animal' applies equally to man and horse, both of which are mortal. This satisfies the unity condition. Now consider defining 'angel' as 'immortal spirit'. Here 'immortal' is a concomitant, not a genus, because spirits (presumably mortal and immortal) differ in essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And among the topics also is when one makes the conceptual genus for a non-constitutive affection, like someone saying that absence of death is eternal life. For eternal life is a thing that is followed, necessitated, and accidentally attached to it is absence of death. And it is not in the right way; for a single life may have been described as absent of death after it was not described as such. Commentary
This passage warns against making a conceptual concomitant (like eternal life) the genus for an accidental property (like absence of death). The example: someone says 'absence of death is eternal life'. But eternal life is a necessary concomitant of life itself; it is not a genus. Moreover, if eternal life were the genus for absence of death, then the genus would have to be one and the same in number across all its species, but it would then apply to both mortal and immortal things, which differ in essence. This is impossible because a genus is predicated uniformly of its species. Additionally, a single life can acquire the property of being death-free after not having it, which shows that absence of death is accidental and not essential to life. Hence, such a definition is invalid.

Key Takeaway

Making an Accident the Genus: The Wind Fallacy
Author refutes defining wind as moving air, showing it mistakes an accident for a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error of making an accidental property the genus for its subject and reconstruct the correct genus-differentia structure.

Core Idea
An accident cannot be the genus of its substance; wind is movement in air, not moving air as a species.

In logic, definitions require careful assignment of genus and differentia. A common mistake is to treat an accidental property—like movement—as if it were the genus of the substance that undergoes it. The classical example of wind illustrates this. As you study the passage, you will see why the definition 'wind is moving air' fails and how to correct it by properly placing 'moving' as the genus and 'from air' as the differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken definition
The false definition under critique is 'wind is moving air' (الريح هواء متحرك). Write it down and note the apparent genus (air) and differentia (moving).
This definition is common in everyday language but logically flawed.
2
Analyze the author's objection
The author argues that if 'air' were the genus of wind, then wind would be a species of air. But air remains the same individual whether it is wind or not, so it cannot be a species. A species does not change genus.
The key test: a species, when redefined, cannot be the same individual as before under a different genus.
3
Identify the actual relationship
Wind is not a substance but an accident (movement) occurring in air. Therefore, the proper logical analysis treats 'moving' as the genus (the broad category of events) and 'from air' as the differentia that specifies this movement.
Thus the correct structure is: wind = moving (genus) that originates from air (differentia).
4
Apply the rule to detect similar fallacies
Whenever a definition uses an accidental property (like movement, color, action) as the genus for a substance, the same error occurs. The genus must be the broader category to which the definiendum essentially belongs.
Test: can the definiendum exist without the supposed genus? Here, wind cannot exist without air, but air can exist without wind, so air is not the genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المواضع أيضًا ما يكون على عكس هذا وهو أن يجعل الانفعال جنسًا للمنفعل؛ وقد كان مثاله في التعليم هو أنه من جعل الريح هواء متحركا ويوهم أن منعه من جهة أن الريح لا يكون هواء متحركا بل هو حركة؛ فظاهر الحال فيه يوهم أن الهواء لا يجب أن يجعل جنسًا للريح. وإذا أخذ هذا على هذا الظاهر لم يكن الانفعال جعل جنسًا للمنفعل بل المنفعل جعل جنسًا للانفعال فسببه أن يكون معناه أن يجعل الريح جنسًا للهواء المتحرك حتى يكون الهواء نوعا من الريح. وتفسير المثال مما يوضحه فإنه قيل. ولا يجوز أن يكون الهواء ريحًا أصلا. وذلك لأن الهواء يبقى واحدًا بالعدد ريحًا وغير ريح والنوع لا يبقى شخصه واحدًا بالعدد، ويخرج من جنسه إلى جنس آخر. فسببه أن يكون الريح جعل في المثال جنسًا للهواء المتحرّك؛ وإنما هو في الحقيقة عرض في الهواء لأنه حركة فيه أو لأنه متحرك منه أي من الهواء فكأنه قال يجب أن يكون الريح ليس هواء متحركا بل متحرّكا هو هواء حتى يكون جنس الريح المتحرّك وفصله أنه من هواء. ثم إن كان قول القائل إن الهواء نوع من الريح يبطل بوجوه أخرى من أن الهواء كيف يكون نوعا من الريح؛ والريح لا يوجد غير هواء: فليس ذلك مانعا أن يبطل أيضًا من هذا الوجه. وليست هذه المواضع الإبطالية بعدة نحو الأشياء التي لا يوجد لإبطالها موضع غيرها بل أن يكون أحد المواضع هي . فلو قال قائل إن الهواء نوع من الريح فإنه قد يبطل بأن يقال إن الهواء يبقى متحرّكا وغير متحرّك، وريحا وغير ريح وهو واحد بالعدد؛ فكان ذلك إبطالًا أيضًا.
And among the topics also is what is the reverse of this, namely making the affection a genus for the subject. Its example in the teaching is that someone who makes wind to be moving air, and it is imagined that its prevention is because wind is not moving air but rather is movement. So the apparent situation suggests that air should not be made a genus for wind. But if this is taken in this apparent sense, then it would not be making the affection a genus for the subject, but rather making the subject a genus for the affection. So the cause is that its meaning is to make wind a genus for moving air, such that air becomes a species of wind. And the interpretation of the example clarifies it, for it is said: 'It is not permissible for air to be wind at all.' That is because air remains one in number as wind and not wind, while a species does not remain its individual one in number, and it goes out from its genus to another genus. So the cause is that wind in the example is made a genus for moving air; but in reality it is an accident in air because it is movement in it, or because it is moving from it, i.e., from air. So it is as if he said: 'Wind must not be moving air, but rather moving that is air, so that the genus of wind is the moving, and its differentia is that it is from air.' Then, if someone says that air is a species of wind, it is invalidated by other aspects: how can air be a species of wind when wind does not exist without air? But that does not prevent it from being invalidated from this aspect as well. And these invalidating topics are not like those things for which there is no other invalidating instance, but rather one of them is the instance. So if someone says that air is a species of wind, it can be invalidated by saying that air remains moving and non-moving, and wind and non-wind, while being one in number; so that is also an invalidation.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage discusses the reverse fallacy of making an affection (movement) the genus for its subject (air). The example: defining wind as 'moving air'. The apparent objection is that wind is not moving air but rather movement itself. However, the real error is that if we treat wind as a genus, then air would be a species of wind, which is absurd because air can be wind or not wind while remaining the same individual. The correct analysis is that wind is an accident (movement) occurring in air. Thus the proper definition would have 'moving' as the genus and 'from air' as the differentia. The author also addresses a counterclaim: if someone argues that air is a species of wind, it is invalidated because air can be both moving and non-moving, wind and non-wind, while being one in number, which a species cannot. Therefore, the definition 'wind is moving air' is logically flawed.

Key Terms
انفعال (inf'āl): affection or accident
منفعل (munfa'il): subject of affection
ريح (rīh): wind
هواء (hawā'): air
متحرك (mutaharrik): moving

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it invalid to say 'air is a species of wind'?
A) Because wind is always moving, while air is not.
B) Because air remains one in number whether it is wind or not, but a species cannot remain the same individual under different genera.
C) Because air is not made of any substance.
D) Because wind is a genus, so air cannot be a species.

Key Takeaway
When defining, ensure the genus is the broader essential category, not an accidental property of the subject; otherwise, the definition commits a logical fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the logical error in the definition: 'Salah is a sequence of physical movements and recitations.' Is the accident 'sequence' being made the genus? Propose a more accurate definition.
?

The Condition for a Real Genus: Predication, Not Subjection
Author specifies predicate condition for real genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given term can be a real genus based on whether it is predicated of the subject and not subject to it.

Core Idea
True genus is always a predicate, never a subject.

In Islamic logic, the concept of genus (jins) is precise. This passage warns against mistaking common usage for reality. Aristotle's examples show that what is commonly called a genus may fail the condition of predication. Let's examine that condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic examples
The author lists examples where common usage treats a term as a genus: 'man is predicated of writer', 'sound is predicated of letter', 'letter is predicated of utterance'. Note these are considered genera in common usage.
2
Extract the condition for real genus
The passage states that a thing cannot be a genus in reality unless it is predicated of that thing (محمول) and not the subject (موضوع). In other words, the genus is a predicate of the species, not the other way around.
For example, in 'all men are animals', 'animal' is predicated of 'man', so 'animal' is a candidate for real genus.
3
Test the condition on an Islamic example
Consider the statement: 'Every khamr is an intoxicant.' Here, 'intoxicant' is predicated of 'khamr', and 'khamr' is not predicated of 'intoxicant'. Therefore, 'intoxicant' satisfies the condition for a real genus of 'khamr'.
4
Recognise failure of common usage examples
Now test the common usage examples: 'man' is not predicated of all 'writers' (not all writers are men), and 'writer' is predicated of 'man' (some men are writers). So 'man' fails the condition of being a predicate; it is actually a subject. Thus it is not a real genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الموضوعات يصدق على الجملة في المشهور هو مثل الإنسان على الكاتب، والصوت على الحرف، والحرف على اللفظ؛ فإنه يشكل فيها الأمر فيظن أجناسا. والمعلم الأوّل إنما أورد أمثال هذه الأشياء بحسب الشهرة مكان الأجناس (١)، ولا يلزم من كون الشيء متصفًا بالشيء بحسب الشهرة أن يكون متصفّا بذلك الشيء في الحقيقة. والشيء لا يمكن أن يكون جنسا للشيء في الحقيقة إلا وأن يكون محمولاً على ذلك الشيء لا موضوعا له. والجدلي يجعل الشيء جنسًا من جهة أنه لازم للشيء ومنعكس عليه؛ فإن ما يكون كذلك فذلك يعد من الأجناس في المشهور. فإذا سلم ذلك فقد ظهر إنتاجه؛ ولا مجال للمنع. وقد يجعل الانعكاس جنسا كذلك لما أنه يعد من الأجناس في المشهور فيجعل المنقسم جنسًا للعدد. وعلى هذا في النظائر فإن من الأشياء ما يكون شبيها بالشيء فيظن أنه يكون ذلك بالحقيقة؛ وذلك قطعي عند الأكثر، ومشهور عندهم. والتعرُّض لذكر كل واحد منها على سبيل التمثيل يفضي إلى التطويل بل إلى التعطيل عند أهل التحصيل.
Then, among the topics that are true of the whole in common usage is like 'man is predicated of writer', and 'sound is predicated of letter', and 'letter is predicated of utterance'; for the matter is problematic in them so they are thought to be genera. And the First Teacher only adduced examples of such things according to common usage in place of genera, and it does not necessarily follow from something being described by something according to common usage that it is described by that thing in reality. And a thing cannot be a genus for something in reality unless it is predicated of that thing and not subject to it. And the dialectician makes a thing a genus by virtue of it being a necessary concomitant of the thing and convertible with it; for whatever is like that is counted among the genera in common usage. So if that is conceded, its product appears, and there is no room for denial. And he may make conversion a genus likewise because it is counted among genera in common usage, so he makes the divisive a genus for number. And likewise in similar cases, for among things there is what is similar to something so it is thought to be that in reality; and that is certain to most people, and common among them. And attempting to mention each one of them by way of example leads to prolixity, indeed to paralysis for the people of attainment.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion about how certain topics are commonly considered genera but are not true genera in reality. The author begins by giving examples from common usage: 'man is predicated of writer', 'sound is predicated of letter', 'letter is predicated of utterance'. These are problematic because they appear to be genera but in reality they are not. The First Teacher (Aristotle) only used these examples based on common convention, not on reality. The key point is that something being described by something in common usage does not imply a real description. In reality, for a thing to be a genus of another, it must be predicated of that thing as a subject, not be the subject itself. For example, in fiqh, 'intoxicant' is a genus of 'khamr' because it is predicated of it. The dialectician, however, treats something as a genus if it is a necessary concomitant (لازم) and convertible with the thing, because that counts as a genus in common opinion. Thus, if such a relation is accepted, it can produce a dialectical argument. The author also notes that conversion itself can be considered a genus in common usage, as when 'divisible' is made a genus for 'number'. This shows that many things are mistakenly thought to be identical to what they resemble. The author concludes that giving a full list would be too lengthy and even counterproductive for serious learners.

Key Terms
المشهور
الجدلي
لازم
منعكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for something to be a real genus according to the passage?
A) It must be widely used in common speech.
B) It must be predicated of the thing and not subject to it.
C) It must be a necessary concomitant convertible with the thing.
D) It must be known by the First Teacher.

Key Takeaway
A real genus must be a predicate of the subject, never the subject itself.

Dialectical Genera: How Common Usage Defines Genera
Author outlines dialectical genus criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a term is treated as a genus in common usage based on necessary concomitance and convertibility.

Core Idea
Dialecticians accept as genus anything that is a necessary concomitant and convertible with the thing.

In dialectical debates, not everyone requires the strict real genus condition. The passage explains that dialecticians (ahl al-jadal) rely on common usage and treat as genera things that are necessary concomitants (lāzim) and convertible (munʿakis). This is acceptable for dialectical arguments, even if it does not meet the real genus standard.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the dialectician's approach
The dialectician does not require real predication; instead, they use common acceptance (شهرة). The passage says the dialectician makes a thing a genus based on it being a necessary concomitant (لازم) and convertible (منعكس) with the thing.
Necessary concomitant means the term always accompanies the thing; convertible means the thing also always accompanies the term in some way.
2
Identify the criterion: lāzim and munʿakis
If a term always goes with the thing and they are interchangeable in common attribution, then that term is considered a genus in common usage. For example, 'divisible' is a necessary concomitant of 'number' (all numbers are divisible) and convertible (divisible things are usually numbers).
3
See the example of conversion as genus
The author gives an example: 'divisible' is made a genus for 'number' in common usage because it is convertible. This shows that even relations like convertibility can be treated as genera in dialectical contexts.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
In kalam, 'temporally originated' (ḥādith) is often considered a genus of the world in common usage, because all temporal things are temporally originated and everything temporally originated is temporal (convertible). Thus, dialecticians accept it as a genus for argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الموضوعات يصدق على الجملة في المشهور هو مثل الإنسان على الكاتب، والصوت على الحرف، والحرف على اللفظ؛ فإنه يشكل فيها الأمر فيظن أجناسا. والمعلم الأوّل إنما أورد أمثال هذه الأشياء بحسب الشهرة مكان الأجناس (١)، ولا يلزم من كون الشيء متصفًا بالشيء بحسب الشهرة أن يكون متصفّا بذلك الشيء في الحقيقة. والشيء لا يمكن أن يكون جنسا للشيء في الحقيقة إلا وأن يكون محمولاً على ذلك الشيء لا موضوعا له. والجدلي يجعل الشيء جنسًا من جهة أنه لازم للشيء ومنعكس عليه؛ فإن ما يكون كذلك فذلك يعد من الأجناس في المشهور. فإذا سلم ذلك فقد ظهر إنتاجه؛ ولا مجال للمنع. وقد يجعل الانعكاس جنسا كذلك لما أنه يعد من الأجناس في المشهور فيجعل المنقسم جنسًا للعدد. وعلى هذا في النظائر فإن من الأشياء ما يكون شبيها بالشيء فيظن أنه يكون ذلك بالحقيقة؛ وذلك قطعي عند الأكثر، ومشهور عندهم. والتعرُّض لذكر كل واحد منها على سبيل التمثيل يفضي إلى التطويل بل إلى التعطيل عند أهل التحصيل.
Then, among the topics that are true of the whole in common usage is like 'man is predicated of writer', and 'sound is predicated of letter', and 'letter is predicated of utterance'; for the matter is problematic in them so they are thought to be genera. And the First Teacher only adduced examples of such things according to common usage in place of genera, and it does not necessarily follow from something being described by something according to common usage that it is described by that thing in reality. And a thing cannot be a genus for something in reality unless it is predicated of that thing and not subject to it. And the dialectician makes a thing a genus by virtue of it being a necessary concomitant of the thing and convertible with it; for whatever is like that is counted among the genera in common usage. So if that is conceded, its product appears, and there is no room for denial. And he may make conversion a genus likewise because it is counted among genera in common usage, so he makes the divisive a genus for number. And likewise in similar cases, for among things there is what is similar to something so it is thought to be that in reality; and that is certain to most people, and common among them. And attempting to mention each one of them by way of example leads to prolixity, indeed to paralysis for the people of attainment.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion about how certain topics are commonly considered genera but are not true genera in reality. The author begins by giving examples from common usage: 'man is predicated of writer', 'sound is predicated of letter', 'letter is predicated of utterance'. These are problematic because they appear to be genera but in reality they are not. The First Teacher (Aristotle) only used these examples based on common convention, not on reality. The key point is that something being described by something in common usage does not imply a real description. In reality, for a thing to be a genus of another, it must be predicated of that thing as a subject, not be the subject itself. For example, in fiqh, 'intoxicant' is a genus of 'khamr' because it is predicated of it. The dialectician, however, treats something as a genus if it is a necessary concomitant (لازم) and convertible with the thing, because that counts as a genus in common opinion. Thus, if such a relation is accepted, it can produce a dialectical argument. The author also notes that conversion itself can be considered a genus in common usage, as when 'divisible' is made a genus for 'number'. This shows that many things are mistakenly thought to be identical to what they resemble. The author concludes that giving a full list would be too lengthy and even counterproductive for serious learners.

Key Terms
المشهور
الجدلي
لازم
منعكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes a thing a genus in common usage?
A) It is predicated as a subject.
B) It is a necessary concomitant and convertible.
C) It is used by Aristotle.
D) It is certain to most people.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical logic, a necessary concomitant and convertible relation suffices to treat something as a genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, one says: 'The world is contingent; contingency is a necessary concomitant of the world and convertible. Therefore, contingency is a genus of the world in common usage.' Is this valid according to the passage? Why?

Condition of Property: Must Be More Known
Author states the condition for a useful property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given property satisfies the condition of being more known than the thing it defines.

Core Idea
A defining property must be better known than the thing defined.

A property (خاصة) is used to clarify an unknown thing. But if the property itself is obscure, it fails its purpose. The author explains this condition and gives examples: fire defined via the soul, which is less known, or via the soul's attachment, which is harder to affirm. To be useful for demonstration, the property must be more known in both conception (تصور) and assent (تصديق).

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of property
A property is a permanent convertible concomitant used to know the unknown. For it to work, it must be more known than the thing it defines.
2
Extract the condition from the passage
The author states that everything known when the thing is unknown is more known than the thing. So the property must satisfy this: it is known while the thing is not.
This is a foundational condition.
3
Analyze the two failing scenarios
The author gives two cases where the property fails: (1) the property's concept is more hidden (e.g., soul is less known than fire), (2) the property's existence for the thing is more hidden (e.g., the attachment of soul to fire is less known than fire itself). Recognize these as violations of the condition.
The difference: first is about conception, second about assent.
4
Apply the condition to a new example
For a property to be useful in demonstration, it must be more known in both conception and assent. Test an example from Islamic sciences, such as 'water is an element that quenches thirst' – is 'quenches thirst' more known than water?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في المواضع المخصوصة بالخاصة وأمثالها. فننظر في هذه المواضع أنها أعم من الخاصة المفردة والمركبة والرسم، وأنها هي على ما عرفته من أقسامها أو التي هي كذا وكذا؛ والتي نبحث هنا عن مواضعها فهي الدائمة المساوية التي يعرف بها المجهول. فمن المواضع المعدَّة نحو الخاصة مواضع تشترك في اعتبار واحد؛ وهو أنه فصل وضعت الخاصة جيدة معتبراً فيها الجودة دون الصدق والكذب. وهو اعتبار أنه هل وقع عندما الشيء مجهول فلا يعرف به الشيء، وكل ما هو معروف عندما الشيء مجهول فهو أعرف من الشيء. فموضع من تلك المواضع أن يكون الشيء المعرف به الأمر على أنها خاصة هو أخفى من الشيء في نفسه وإن كان موجودًا للشيء؛ وليس يتعرف بالشيء مثل أن تقول إن النار جرم يشبه النفس لطافة. ثمَّ النفس وإن كانت لا تعرف بالنار بقوة ولا فعل لها فإنها أخفى من النار. وموضع آخر أن يكون وجود الخاصة للمخصوص أخفى من معرفة ذات المخصوص مثل من يعرف النار أو الحار بأنه الذي يتعلق به النفس أولا وتصديقنا بتعلق النفس بالنار أخفى من تصورنا للنار. والفرق بين هذا الموضع والأول أن الأول كان الأخفى فيه هو تصور من تصور؛ وهنا تصديق من تصور وكل واحد من هذين الموضعين تعليمي أيضًا؛ وللإبطال. وأما للإثبات فلا يكون إلا وأن يكون بعد أن صحت المساواة في الانعكاس قد بان أنها أعرف من الوجهين أعني التصديق والتصور؛ فينتفع في الإثبات. ويجب أن تعلم أن من الخاصة ما هو أعرف بالذات من المخصوص كالحركة إلى فوق والإضاءة؛ فإنها أعرف بالذات من طبيعة النار الحقيقية بالقياس إلى أوهامنا، ومنه ما صار أعرف بالنظر مثل كون الزاوية الخارجة أعظم من كل واحدة من الداخلتين المتقابلتين خاصة لمساوي الزوايا القائمتين، وأعرف منه؛ وبها يعرف ما ليس أعرف منه ولكن عرف بالنظر أنه يخصه مثل كون الزوايا مساوية لقائمتين في الشكل المثلث، فإذا أوردت دلت على الشيء. والأولى أن يخص مثل هذا باسم الرسم ما كان يعرف ما هو أخفى منه إما في معناه؛ وذلك ظاهر. وأما بحسب اسمه حتى يكون الاسم إذا ذكر لم يفهم فيدل مفهومه الخاصة. وإن كان معنى الاسم سابقا إلى التصور وأسبق من الرسم كما إذا قيل مثلث فلم يفهم فيعرف بأنه شكل زواياه مساوية لقائمتين ففهم حينئذ. وإن كان معنى الاسم معلوما فلا يكون كذلك؛ إذ الرسم إنما
Chapter: On the special topics of the property and their likes. We look into these topics, that they are more general than the simple property, the composite, and the delimited description, and that they are as you have known from their divisions or which are such and such; and those whose topics we investigate here are the permanent convertible ones by which the unknown is known. Among the topics prepared for the property are topics that share in one consideration, namely that a property has been placed well, with quality considered in it regardless of truth and falsehood. And it is the consideration of whether when the thing is unknown it is not known by the thing, and everything that is known when the thing is unknown is more known than the thing. One of those topics is that the thing by which something is known as a property is more hidden than the thing itself, even if it exists for the thing; and it is not recognized by the thing, as if you say: fire is a body resembling the soul in subtlety. Then the soul, even if it is not known by fire in power or action, is more hidden than fire. Another topic is that the existence of the property for the particular is more hidden than the knowledge of the essence of the particular, like one who knows fire or the hot as that which is first attached to the soul, and our affirmation of the attachment of the soul to fire is more hidden than our conception of fire. The difference between this topic and the first is that in the first what was more hidden was a conception of a conception, and here it is an affirmation of a conception. And each of these two topics is also instructional, and for refutation. As for demonstration, it only occurs after the equality in conversion has been established and it has become clear that it is more known from both aspects, namely affirmation and conception, so it is useful in demonstration. And you must know that among the property there is what is more known by essence than the particular, like motion upward and illumination, for they are more known by essence than the real nature of fire relative to our estimations; and from it there is what has become more known by consideration, like the external angle being greater than each of the two opposite interior angles being a property of the triangle equal to two right angles, and it is more known; and by it is known what is not more known, but it is known by consideration that it belongs to it, like the angles being equal to two right angles in a triangular shape, so if it is adduced it indicates the thing. And it is more appropriate to call such a thing a delimiting description (rasm) that which knows what is more hidden than it, either in its meaning; that is clear. As for with regard to its name, so that the name, if mentioned, is not understood, then its concept indicates the property. And if the meaning of the name is prior to conception and previous to the delimiting description, as when it is said 'triangle' and it is not understood, then it is known by being a shape whose angles are equal to two right angles, then it is understood at that time. And if the meaning of the name is known, it is not like that; for the delimiting description only...
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory discussion of the special topics (مواضع) that pertain to the logical property (خاصة) and similar concepts. The author begins by stating that these topics are more general than the simple property, the composite property, and the delimiting description (الرسم). The specific topics under investigation here are the 'permanent convertible' ones that are used to define the unknown. Among the topics prepared for properties, there is a common consideration: that a property is considered well if it has quality regardless of truth or falsehood; the key is whether when the thing is unknown, it is not known by that property, and that property is more known than the thing. The author then gives two specific topics (موضعان). First: the property used to define is more hidden than the thing itself, even though it exists for the thing. For example, saying 'fire is a body resembling the soul in subtlety' – the soul is less known than fire, so this property (resembling the soul) fails to clarify fire. Second: the existence of the property for the particular is more hidden than knowledge of the essence of the particular. For instance, defining fire or the hot as 'that which the soul attaches itself to first' – our affirmation that the soul attaches to fire is less known than our conception of fire. The difference between the two: in the first, the more hidden element is a conception of a conception; in the second, it is an affirmation of a conception. Both are instructional and used for refutation, but for demonstration, the property must be more known in both conception and affirmation after establishing convertible equality. Then the author explains that some properties are more known by essence (e.g., 'motion upward' and 'illumination' are more known than the true nature of fire), while others become more known by consideration (e.g., the external angle being greater than the opposite interior angles is a property of the triangle that is more known than the equality of angles to two right angles, and by it one knows the latter). The author recommends reserving the term 'rasm' (delimiting description) for cases where something reveals what is more hidden. Finally, there is a discussion about whether the name itself is understood: if the name is not understood, the concept of the property can indicate it; if the meaning of the name is prior and already known, then the delimiting description is not needed. The passage ends mid-sentence suggesting further explanation.

خاصة
تصديق
تصور
مساواة

A property used for definition must be more known than the thing it defines in both conception and assent.

Two Faulty Topics for Property: Hidden in Concept vs. Hidden in Assent
Author distinguishes two ways property can be more hidden

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a property that is more hidden in conception and one that is more hidden in assent, using the examples from the passage.

Core Idea
A property fails if its concept is obscure or if its existence is hard to affirm.

The author details two specific ways a property can be more hidden than the thing it defines. In the first, the property's concept (تصور) is less known; in the second, the property's existence (تصديق) for the thing is less known. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two failing topics
The author lists two topics (موضعان) where a property is more hidden: (1) the property's own concept is more hidden, (2) the property's existence for the thing is more hidden.
These correspond to defects in conception (تصور) and assent (تصديق).
2
Examine the first topic: fire and soul
Example: 'fire is a body resembling the soul in subtlety.' The soul is less known than fire, so the property (resembling the soul) is more hidden in concept. This is the first type: hiddenness in conception.
The property itself is not well-known.
3
Examine the second topic: fire and soul's attachment
Example: 'fire is that which the soul first attaches to.' Here, the property's existence (that the soul attaches to fire) is harder to affirm than the concept of fire itself. This is hiddenness in assent.
The difference is between lacking knowledge of the concept vs. lacking certainty of the relation.
4
Apply the distinction to a new example
Take the property 'the world is created because it is composite.' Is 'being composite' more hidden in concept or in assent relative to 'world'? Consider both aspects.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For each of the following properties, determine whether the hiddenness is in conception or assent: (a) 'Man is a rational animal' – is rationality more hidden in concept or in existence? (b) 'The Quran is miraculous because it is inimitable' – is inimitability more hidden in concept or in proof of its existence?
?
Consider a jurist defining riba (usury) as 'an increase without equivalent in a barter of weighed items'. Is the property 'increase without equivalent' more hidden in conception or in assenting to its presence? Explain using the two topics.

Defects of Obscurity in Definitions by Property
Author identifies and exemplifies defects in definitions using properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a property in a definition by property (rasm) is more obscure than the definiendum and distinguish between obscurity in conception and in affirmation.

Core Idea
A definition by property fails if the property is less known than the thing it defines.

In defining entities by their properties, logicians require that the property be more known (a'raf) than the definiendum. This passage outlines two ways a property can be more obscure: the property itself may be less known, or its existence for the subject may be less known. Understanding this distinction helps in evaluating the quality of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and property
Find the thing being defined (e.g., fire) and the property used (e.g., resembling the soul).
2
Assess relative clarity
Determine if the property is more known (a'raf) or more obscure (akhfa) than the definiendum.
3
Classify the type of obscurity
Is the obscurity in the conception of the property itself (tasawwur) or in the affirmation of its existence for the subject (tasdiq)?
The passage gives examples: first case obscure in conception, second case obscure in affirmation.
4
Check for conversion equality
If the property is co-extensive and becomes more known after conversion, it may be used for proof.
5
Distinguish properties by source of knownness
Some properties are more known in essence (like upward motion for fire), others become known by consideration (like external angle property for triangle).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيء مجهول فهو أعرف من الشيء. فموضع من تلك المواضع أن يكون الشيء المعرف به الأمر على أنها خاصة هو أخفى من الشيء في نفسه وإن كان موجودًا للشيء؛ وليس يتعرف بالشيء مثل أن تقول إن النار جرم يشبه النفس لطافة. ثمَّ النفس وإن كانت لا تعرف بالنار بقوة ولا فعل لها فإنها أخفى من النار. وموضع آخر أن يكون وجود الخاصة للمخصوص أخفى من معرفة ذات المخصوص مثل من يعرف النار أو الحار بأنه الذي يتعلق به النفس أولا وتصديقنا بتعلق النفس بالنار أخفى من تصورنا للنار. والفرق بين هذا الموضع والأول أن الأول كان الأخفى فيه هو تصور من تصور؛ وهنا تصديق من تصور وكل واحد من هذين الموضعين تعليمي أيضًا؛ وللإبطال. وأما للإثبات فلا يكون إلا وأن يكون بعد أن صحت المساواة في الانعكاس قد بان أنها أعرف من الوجهين أعني التصديق والتصور؛ فينتفع في الإثبات. ويجب أن تعلم أن من الخاصة ما هو أعرف بالذات من المخصوص كالحركة إلى فوق والإضاءة؛ فإنها أعرف بالذات من طبيعة النار الحقيقية بالقياس إلى أوهامنا، ومنه ما صار أعرف بالنظر مثل كون الزاوية الخارجة أعظم من كل واحدة من الداخلتين المتقابلتين خاصة لمساوي الزوايا القائمتين، وأعرف منه؛ وبها يعرف ما ليس أعرف منه ولكن عرف بالنظر أنه يخصه مثل كون الزوايا مساوية لقائمتين في الشكل المثلث، فإذا أوردت دلت على الشيء. والأولى أن يخص مثل هذا باسم الرسم ما كان يعرف ما هو أخفى منه إما في معناه؛ وذلك ظاهر. وأما بحسب اسمه حتى يكون الاسم إذا ذكر لم يفهم فيدل مفهومه الخاصة. وإن كان معنى الاسم سابقا إلى التصور وأسبق من الرسم كما إذا قيل مثلث فلم يفهم فيعرف بأنه شكل زواياه مساوية لقائمتين ففهم حينئذ. وإن كان معنى الاسم معلوما فلا يكون كذلك؛ إذ الرسم إنما يحتاج إليه لتعريف المجهول لا من أمر ذاتي بل بعلامة؛ وهذا موضع فرق بين الخاصة المركبة وبين الرسم.
ومن هذه المواضع أن يكون القول ليس يشتمل على اسم مشترك مشكل فإنه إذا اشتمل على ذلك لم تكن الخاصة جيدة كمن يقول إن الحيوان خاصته أن يحس ؛ ثم لا يُدرى أنه يحس بالفعل أو بالقوة. والأول كاذب لأنه لا ينعكس، والثاني صادق. وقد يكون هذا الإشكال تارة بحسب اشتراك خاص بالمفرد الداخل في جملة القول، وقد يكون بحسب الاشتراك الواقع في تركيب القول ؛ وكلاهما غير جيد. وكيف والخاصة التي كلامنا فيها يراد بها التعريف واللفظ المشترك في كل واحد منهما مما يزيد الأمر إشكالا. وما يكون الاعتبار فيه من جهة المخصوص كما إذا كان اسمه مشتركا لم يدل على أن الخاصة لأي معنى من معانيه؛ فذلك يجري مجرى الموضع الأول.
ومن المواضع المتعلقة بالجودة والرداءة أن يكون في القول تكرار كمن يقول إن خاصة النار أنها جسم ألطف الأجسام؛ فإن ألطف الأجسام لا يكون إلا جسما : ففيه تكرار بالفعل. ولو قلت إن الأرض جوهر من الأجسام ينتقل بالطبع إلى أسفل؛ ففيه تكرار بالقوة فإن الجوهر في ضمن الجسم الذي أخذ فيه. ويشبه أن يكون المثال المشترك للمشهور وللحق في هذا أن لو قيل النار جسم ما من الأجسام هو : فإن قوله جسم ما مع قوله من الأجسام هزر وتكرار من غير حاجة؛ فإن قوله جسم ما هو من الأجسام. وأما قوله جوهر من الأجسام فإنه جدلي وتعليمي معا فكأنه قال جوهر ما من الجواهر؛ إذ الأجسام في الحقيقة جواهر. وقوله جسم من أجسام كذا كان تكرارًا بالفعل كذلك؛ وهو قوله جوهر ما من الأجسام والأجسام بماهياتها وحدودها تتم بأنها جواهر فيكون كأنه قال جوهر ما من الجواهر؛ فيكون تكرارًا بالقوة. وأما قوله إن الإنسان حيوان قابل
The thing is unknown, so it is known more than the thing. So one of those positions is that the thing by which [something] is defined, as being a property, is more hidden than the thing itself, even if it exists for the thing; and it is not known by the thing, such as saying 'fire is a body resembling the soul in subtlety.' Then the soul, even if it is not known by the fire, nor has power or action, is more hidden than fire. Another position is that the existence of the property for the thing qualified is more hidden than knowing the essence of the qualified, such as one who knows fire or hot as that to which the soul attaches first, and our affirmation of the attachment of the soul to fire is more hidden than our conception of fire. The difference between this position and the first is that the first [case] had the more hidden being a conception from a conception; here it is an affirmation from a conception. And each of these two positions is also instructional; and for refutation. As for for proof, it is only when equality in conversion has been established, it becomes clear that it is more known from both aspects, i.e., affirmation and conception; so it benefits in proof. And you must know that some properties are more known in essence than the qualified, like upward motion and illumination; for they are more known in essence than the real nature of fire relative to our estimations. And some become more known by consideration, such as the external angle being greater than each of the two opposite interior angles is a property of [triangle] equality to two right angles, and more known than it; and by it is known what is not more known than it but is known by consideration that it belongs to it, like the angles being equal to two right angles in the triangle shape. So when it is applied, it indicates the thing. And it is better to reserve the name 'rasm' for what defines what is more hidden than it, either in its meaning; and that is clear. And as for according to its name, so that the name, when mentioned, is not understood [then] the concept of the property indicates it. And if the meaning of the name is prior to the conception and more prior than the rasm, as when 'triangle' is said and not understood, then it is defined as a shape whose angles equal two right angles, so then it is understood. And if the meaning of the name is known, it is not so; for the rasm is only needed to define the unknown, not by an essential matter but by a sign; and this is a place of difference between the composite property and the rasm. And among these positions is that the statement does not include an ambiguous problematic term, for if it includes that, the property is not good, like one who says 'the property of animal is that it senses'; then it is not known whether it senses actually or potentially. The first is false because it is not convertible, and the second is true. And this problem is sometimes due to ambiguity specific to the individual word included in the statement, and sometimes due to the ambiguity occurring in the composition of the statement; and both are not good. How so, when the property we are discussing is intended for definition, and the ambiguous term in each of them increases the problem. And what is considered from the aspect of the qualified, as when its name is ambiguous and does not indicate which of its meanings the property is for; that runs in the manner of the first position. And among the positions related to goodness and badness is that there is repetition in the statement, like one who says 'the property of fire is that it is a body the most subtle of bodies'; for the most subtle of bodies is only a body: so there is actual repetition. And if you said 'earth is a substance of the bodies that by nature moves downward'; there is potential repetition because substance is within body which is taken in it. And it seems the common example for the famous and the truth in this is if it were said 'fire is a body from the bodies is it'? for his saying 'a body what' along with his saying 'from the bodies' is nonsense and repetition without need; for his saying 'a body what' is 'from the bodies'. As for his saying 'substance from the bodies', it is dialectical and instructional together, as if he said 'a substance from the substances', since bodies are in reality substances. And his saying 'body from bodies such' was actual repetition similarly; and it is his saying 'substance from the bodies', and bodies by their quiddities and definitions are completed as substances, so it is as if he said 'a substance from the substances'; so it is potential repetition. And as for his saying 'man is a rational animal'...
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on defects in definitions by property (al-rasm bi'l-khassa). The author, likely Ibn Sina or a later logician, enumerates several 'positions' (mawadi') that render a definition by property faulty. The discussion is systematic. First, the property may be more obscure (akhfa) than the definiendum itself. Two subcases are given: (i) the property is more hidden in itself, e.g., defining fire by comparing it to the soul (which is even less known), and (ii) the existence of the property for the subject is more hidden than knowing the subject's essence, e.g., defining fire as 'that to which the soul attaches first' — we may conceive fire but the fact of soul-attachment is obscure. The author distinguishes between conception (tasawwur) and affirmation (tasdiq) in these cases. He notes that such definitions may work for teaching or refutation but not for proof unless the property is equally known or better known after conversion. Then he discusses properties that are more known in essence (e.g., upward motion for fire) or become more known by consideration (e.g., the external angle property for a triangle). The second major defect is ambiguity (ishtirak). If the definition contains an ambiguous term, it fails to define clearly. The example is 'animal senses' — ambiguous between actual and potential sensing; the actual sense is false (not convertible), the potential sense true but unclear. The ambiguity may arise from a single word or from the composition of the phrase. Thirdly, redundancy (takrar) is a defect. Two types: actual repetition (e.g., 'fire is a body the most subtle of bodies' — 'body' is repeated needlessly) and potential repetition (e.g., 'earth is a substance of the bodies' — 'substance' is already implied by 'body'). The author gives a detailed analysis of phrases like 'fire is a body from the bodies' as pointless repetition. The final sentence begins another example about 'man is a rational animal' but is cut off. Throughout, the author uses examples from natural philosophy (fire, earth, animal, triangle) typical of the Islamic logical tradition.

Key Terms
خاصة (khassa): A non-essential attribute that belongs to a species and can be used to define it by way of a sign (rasm), as opposed to essential definition (hadd). [Note: In earlier logic (like Aristotle), 'idion' is broader; in Islamic logic, khassa is strictly a co-extensive accident.]
رسم (rasm): A definition that uses properties or accidents rather than essential differentiae; it is a mark or sign to identify the thing. [Note: No significant shift — stable across the tradition.]
تكرار (takrar): Redundancy in a definition, either actual (bil-fi'l, explicit) or potential (bil-quwwa, implicit). [Note: This classification of repetition is common in logic commentaries.]
اشتراك (ishtirak): Ambiguity or equivocation in a term or phrase, making the definition unclear. [Note: Used consistently in logic and usul al-fiqh.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defect in defining fire as 'a body resembling the soul in subtlety'?
A) It contains repetition.
B) The property 'resembling the soul' is more obscure than fire itself.
C) The property is not co-extensive.
D) It uses an ambiguous term.

Key Takeaway
A property used in a definition must be more known than the definiendum; otherwise, the definition fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition 'Wine is an intoxicating beverage.' Identify if the property 'intoxicating' is more known than wine for a layperson. Which type of obscurity (conception or affirmation) does it fall under?
?
Evaluate the definition 'Man is a rational animal.' Is this a definition by property or a complete essential definition? Does it suffer from obscurity? (Use the passage's criteria.)

Defects of Ambiguity and Redundancy in Definitions by Property
Author identifies and exemplifies defects in definitions using properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify ambiguity (ishtirak) and redundancy (takrar) in definitions by property, and distinguish between actual and potential repetition.

Core Idea
A good definition by property must avoid ambiguous terms and unnecessary repetition.

Building on the earlier discussion of obscurity, this part of the passage addresses two additional defects: ambiguity and redundancy. An ambiguous term makes the definition unclear; redundancy (either explicit or implicit) makes it verbose and uninformative. These principles are crucial for crafting precise definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Scan for ambiguous terms
Look for any word or phrase that could have multiple meanings (ishtirak).
Example: 'senses' could mean actively sensing or having the potential to sense.
2
Identify the source of ambiguity
Determine if the ambiguity comes from a single word or from the composition of the statement.
3
Check for redundancy
Look for elements that repeat what is already implied, either explicitly or implicitly.
4
Classify repetition type
If the repetition is explicit, it is actual (bil-fi'l); if implicit, it is potential (bil-quwwa).
Example: 'fire is a body the most subtle of bodies' has actual repetition because 'most subtle of bodies' already implies body.
5
Evaluate the definition's quality
Decide whether the definition achieves its purpose given the defects found.
A definition with ambiguity or redundancy is considered deficient.

ومن هذه المواضع أن يكون القول ليس يشتمل على اسم مشترك مشكل فإنه إذا اشتمل على ذلك لم تكن الخاصة جيدة كمن يقول إن الحيوان خاصته أن يحس ؛ ثم لا يُدرى أنه يحس بالفعل أو بالقوة. والأول كاذب لأنه لا ينعكس، والثاني صادق. وقد يكون هذا الإشكال تارة بحسب اشتراك خاص بالمفرد الداخل في جملة القول، وقد يكون بحسب الاشتراك الواقع في تركيب القول ؛ وكلاهما غير جيد. وكيف والخاصة التي كلامنا فيها يراد بها التعريف واللفظ المشترك في كل واحد منهما مما يزيد الأمر إشكالا. وما يكون الاعتبار فيه من جهة المخصوص كما إذا كان اسمه مشتركا لم يدل على أن الخاصة لأي معنى من معانيه؛ فذلك يجري مجرى الموضع الأول.
ومن المواضع المتعلقة بالجودة والرداءة أن يكون في القول تكرار كمن يقول إن خاصة النار أنها جسم ألطف الأجسام؛ فإن ألطف الأجسام لا يكون إلا جسما : ففيه تكرار بالفعل. ولو قلت إن الأرض جوهر من الأجسام ينتقل بالطبع إلى أسفل؛ ففيه تكرار بالقوة فإن الجوهر في ضمن الجسم الذي أخذ فيه. ويشبه أن يكون المثال المشترك للمشهور وللحق في هذا أن لو قيل النار جسم ما من الأجسام هو : فإن قوله جسم ما مع قوله من الأجسام هزر وتكرار من غير حاجة؛ فإن قوله جسم ما هو من الأجسام. وأما قوله جوهر من الأجسام فإنه جدلي وتعليمي معا فكأنه قال جوهر ما من الجواهر؛ إذ الأجسام في الحقيقة جواهر. وقوله جسم من أجسام كذا كان تكرارًا بالفعل كذلك؛ وهو قوله جوهر ما من الأجسام والأجسام بماهياتها وحدودها تتم بأنها جواهر فيكون كأنه قال جوهر ما من الجواهر؛ فيكون تكرارًا بالقوة. وأما قوله إن الإنسان حيوان قابل
The thing is unknown, so it is known more than the thing. So one of those positions is that the thing by which [something] is defined, as being a property, is more hidden than the thing itself, even if it exists for the thing; and it is not known by the thing, such as saying 'fire is a body resembling the soul in subtlety.' Then the soul, even if it is not known by the fire, nor has power or action, is more hidden than fire. Another position is that the existence of the property for the thing qualified is more hidden than knowing the essence of the qualified, such as one who knows fire or hot as that to which the soul attaches first, and our affirmation of the attachment of the soul to fire is more hidden than our conception of fire. The difference between this position and the first is that the first [case] had the more hidden being a conception from a conception; here it is an affirmation from a conception. And each of these two positions is also instructional; and for refutation. As for for proof, it is only when equality in conversion has been established, it becomes clear that it is more known from both aspects, i.e., affirmation and conception; so it benefits in proof. And you must know that some properties are more known in essence than the qualified, like upward motion and illumination; for they are more known in essence than the real nature of fire relative to our estimations. And some become more known by consideration, such as the external angle being greater than each of the two opposite interior angles is a property of [triangle] equality to two right angles, and more known than it; and by it is known what is not more known than it but is known by consideration that it belongs to it, like the angles being equal to two right angles in the triangle shape. So when it is applied, it indicates the thing. And it is better to reserve the name 'rasm' for what defines what is more hidden than it, either in its meaning; and that is clear. And as for according to its name, so that the name, when mentioned, is not understood [then] the concept of the property indicates it. And if the meaning of the name is prior to the conception and more prior than the rasm, as when 'triangle' is said and not understood, then it is defined as a shape whose angles equal two right angles, so then it is understood. And if the meaning of the name is known, it is not so; for the rasm is only needed to define the unknown, not by an essential matter but by a sign; and this is a place of difference between the composite property and the rasm. And among these positions is that the statement does not include an ambiguous problematic term, for if it includes that, the property is not good, like one who says 'the property of animal is that it senses'; then it is not known whether it senses actually or potentially. The first is false because it is not convertible, and the second is true. And this problem is sometimes due to ambiguity specific to the individual word included in the statement, and sometimes due to the ambiguity occurring in the composition of the statement; and both are not good. How so, when the property we are discussing is intended for definition, and the ambiguous term in each of them increases the problem. And what is considered from the aspect of the qualified, as when its name is ambiguous and does not indicate which of its meanings the property is for; that runs in the manner of the first position. And among the positions related to goodness and badness is that there is repetition in the statement, like one who says 'the property of fire is that it is a body the most subtle of bodies'; for the most subtle of bodies is only a body: so there is actual repetition. And if you said 'earth is a substance of the bodies that by nature moves downward'; there is potential repetition because substance is within body which is taken in it. And it seems the common example for the famous and the truth in this is if it were said 'fire is a body from the bodies is it'? for his saying 'a body what' along with his saying 'from the bodies' is nonsense and repetition without need; for his saying 'a body what' is 'from the bodies'. As for his saying 'substance from the bodies', it is dialectical and instructional together, as if he said 'a substance from the substances', since bodies are in reality substances. And his saying 'body from bodies such' was actual repetition similarly; and it is his saying 'substance from the bodies', and bodies by their quiddities and definitions are completed as substances, so it is as if he said 'a substance from the substances'; so it is potential repetition. And as for his saying 'man is a rational animal'...
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on defects in definitions by property (al-rasm bi'l-khassa). The author, likely Ibn Sina or a later logician, enumerates several 'positions' (mawadi') that render a definition by property faulty. The discussion is systematic. First, the property may be more obscure (akhfa) than the definiendum itself. Two subcases are given: (i) the property is more hidden in itself, e.g., defining fire by comparing it to the soul (which is even less known), and (ii) the existence of the property for the subject is more hidden than knowing the subject's essence, e.g., defining fire as 'that to which the soul attaches first' — we may conceive fire but the fact of soul-attachment is obscure. The author distinguishes between conception (tasawwur) and affirmation (tasdiq) in these cases. He notes that such definitions may work for teaching or refutation but not for proof unless the property is equally known or better known after conversion. Then he discusses properties that are more known in essence (e.g., upward motion for fire) or become more known by consideration (e.g., the external angle property for a triangle). The second major defect is ambiguity (ishtirak). If the definition contains an ambiguous term, it fails to define clearly. The example is 'animal senses' — ambiguous between actual and potential sensing; the actual sense is false (not convertible), the potential sense true but unclear. The ambiguity may arise from a single word or from the composition of the phrase. Thirdly, redundancy (takrar) is a defect. Two types: actual repetition (e.g., 'fire is a body the most subtle of bodies' — 'body' is repeated needlessly) and potential repetition (e.g., 'earth is a substance of the bodies' — 'substance' is already implied by 'body'). The author gives a detailed analysis of phrases like 'fire is a body from the bodies' as pointless repetition. The final sentence begins another example about 'man is a rational animal' but is cut off. Throughout, the author uses examples from natural philosophy (fire, earth, animal, triangle) typical of the Islamic logical tradition.

Key Terms
خاصة (khassa): A non-essential attribute that belongs to a species and can be used to define it by way of a sign (rasm), as opposed to essential definition (hadd). [Note: In earlier logic (like Aristotle), 'idion' is broader; in Islamic logic, khassa is strictly a co-extensive accident.]
رسم (rasm): A definition that uses properties or accidents rather than essential differentiae; it is a mark or sign to identify the thing. [Note: No significant shift — stable across the tradition.]
تكرار (takrar): Redundancy in a definition, either actual (bil-fi'l, explicit) or potential (bil-quwwa, implicit). [Note: This classification of repetition is common in logic commentaries.]
اشتراك (ishtirak): Ambiguity or equivocation in a term or phrase, making the definition unclear. [Note: Used consistently in logic and usul al-fiqh.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the definition 'fire is a body the most subtle of bodies' suffers from which defect?
A) Ambiguity
B) Obscurity
C) Actual repetition
D) Potential repetition

Key Takeaway
Definitions by property must be free from ambiguity and repetition to be effective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The definition 'Zakat is a charitable payment of a fixed portion of one's wealth' may contain potential repetition? Check if 'payment' is already implied by 'charitable' or 'portion.' Identify any redundancy.
?
Consider the definition 'The property of human is that he is a speaking animal.' Does this definition contain ambiguity? Why or why not? (Use the passage's analysis of 'animal senses'.)

Avoiding Ambiguity in Defining Properties
Author discusses the fault of ambiguous terms in property definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify ambiguous terms in a property statement and explain why ambiguity invalidates the definition.

Core Idea
A property must be unambiguous; a shared term can destroy its clarifying power.

In defining properties, clarity is paramount. The author warns that a property statement must not contain a 'problematic shared name' (اسم مشترك مشكل) that leaves the meaning unsettled. For example, saying 'the property of animal is that it senses' is ambiguous: does 'senses' mean actually or potentially? This uncertainty makes the property useless for definition. We will explore how ambiguity arises and why it must be avoided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared term
Look for any term in the property statement that has multiple meanings (ishtirāk). In the example, 'senses' (يحس) can mean actual sensation or potential sensation. Note the ambiguity.
Ambiguous terms are also called 'shared names'.
2
Check the consequence of each meaning
Test both interpretations. If taken actually, the property fails (not all animals are actually sensing). If taken potentially, it is true but ambiguous. The property should be true and clear.
The author says the first is false, the second true but still problematic.
3
Determine the source of ambiguity
Is the ambiguity in a single word or in the composition? The author says both are problematic. Also, if the thing being defined has a shared name, it may not be clear which meaning the property applies to.
4
Evaluate the property's clarity
A good property must be unambiguous. If the statement contains any ambiguous term, it is not a good property. Conclude whether the property is acceptable.
Definition requires clarity; ambiguity defeats the purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذه المواضع أن يكون القول ليس يشتمل على اسم مشترك مشكل فإنه إذا اشتمل على ذلك لم تكن الخاصة جيدة كمن يقول إن الحيوان خاصته أن يحس ؛ ثم لا يُدرى أنه يحس بالفعل أو بالقوة. والأول كاذب لأنه لا ينعكس، والثاني صادق. وقد يكون هذا الإشكال تارة بحسب اشتراك خاص بالمفرد الداخل في جملة القول، وقد يكون بحسب الاشتراك الواقع في تركيب القول ؛ وكلاهما غير جيد. وكيف والخاصة التي كلامنا فيها يراد بها التعريف واللفظ المشترك في كل واحد منهما مما يزيد الأمر إشكالا. وما يكون الاعتبار فيه من جهة المخصوص كما إذا كان اسمه مشتركا لم يدل على أن الخاصة لأي معنى من معانيه؛ فذلك يجري مجرى الموضع الأول.

ومن المواضع المتعلقة بالجودة والرداءة أن يكون في القول تكرار كمن يقول إن خاصة النار أنها جسم ألطف الأجسام؛ فإن ألطف الأجسام لا يكون إلا جسما : ففيه تكرار بالفعل. ولو قلت إن الأرض جوهر من الأجسام ينتقل بالطبع إلى أسفل؛ ففيه تكرار بالقوة فإن الجوهر في ضمن الجسم الذي أخذ فيه. ويشبه أن يكون المثال المشترك للمشهور وللحق في هذا أن لو قيل النار جسم ما من الأجسام هو : فإن قوله جسم ما مع قوله من الأجسام هزر وتكرار من غير حاجة؛ فإن قوله جسم ما هو من الأجسام. وأما قوله جوهر من الأجسام فإنه جدلي وتعليمي معا فكأنه قال جوهر ما من الجواهر؛ إذ الأجسام في الحقيقة جواهر. وقوله جسم من أجسام كذا كان تكرارًا بالفعل كذلك؛ وهو قوله جوهر ما من الأجسام والأجسام بماهياتها وحدودها تتم بأنها جواهر فيكون كأنه قال جوهر ما من الجواهر؛ فيكون تكرارًا بالقوة. وأما قوله إن الإنسان حيوان قابل للعلم فقد قال شيئًا لا تكرار فيه أصلا لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة؛ إذ الحيوان لا يكون في ضمن القابل للعلم. وكذلك القابل للعلم لا يكون في ضمن الحيوان.
Among these topics is that the statement should not include a problematic shared name, for if it includes that, the property is not good, as when one says that the property of animal is that it senses; then it is not known whether it senses actually or potentially. The first is false because it does not convert, and the second is true. This problem sometimes arises due to the ambiguity specific to the singular term included in the statement, and sometimes due to the ambiguity occurring in the composition of the statement; both are not good. How so, while the property we are discussing is intended for definition, and the shared name in each of them increases the problem. And that which is considered from the perspective of the thing qualified, as when its name is shared and does not indicate which of its meanings the property is for; that runs along the lines of the first topic.

Among the topics related to quality and poor quality is that the statement contains repetition, as when one says that the property of fire is that it is the subtlest body; for the subtlest of bodies is nothing but a body: so in it there is actual repetition. And if you said that earth is a substance among bodies that naturally moves downward; then in it there is potential repetition, for substance is implied within body which is taken. And the example common between the well-known and the truth in this is as if one said fire is a body among bodies: for his saying 'a body' with his saying 'among bodies' is nonsense and repetition without need; for his saying 'a body' means it is among bodies. As for his saying 'substance among bodies', it is both dialectical and pedagogical together, as if he said 'a substance among substances', since bodies in reality are substances. And his saying 'body among such bodies' is actual repetition likewise; and it is his saying 'a substance among bodies', and bodies by their quiddities and definitions are complete as being substances, so it is as if he said 'a substance among substances'; so it is potential repetition. As for his saying that man is an animal capable of knowledge, he has said something with no repetition at all, neither actually nor potentially; for animal is not implied within 'capable of knowledge', nor is 'capable of knowledge' implied within animal.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage discusses two faults that render a property (خاصة) defective: ambiguity (اشتراك) and repetition (تكرار). The author first addresses ambiguity: a property statement must not contain a problematic shared name (اسم مشترك مشكل). For example, saying 'the property of animal is that it senses' is ambiguous because 'senses' could mean actual sensation or potential sensation. If taken actually, the statement is false because not all animals are actually sensing (e.g., sleeping animals), and it fails the conversion requirement (انعكاس). If taken potentially, it is true but ambiguous. The ambiguity may arise from the singular term or from the composition; both are problematic because definition requires clarity. The author then turns to repetition, distinguishing actual repetition (تكرار بالفعل) from potential repetition (تكرار بالقوة). Actual repetition occurs when the predicate explicitly repeats part of the subject, as in 'fire is the subtlest body' where 'subtlest body' necessarily implies 'body', so 'body' is repeated. Potential repetition occurs when the predicate implicitly contains the subject, as in 'earth is a substance among bodies' because 'substance' is already included in 'body' (since bodies are substances). A good, repetition-free example is 'man is an animal capable of knowledge': 'animal' and 'capable of knowledge' are not mutually implied, so there is no redundancy. This illustrates the standard for a well-formed property: it must be clear and concise, avoiding both ambiguity and unnecessary repetition.

Key Terms
اشتراك
خاصة
بالفعل / بالقوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the property 'Animal is a sensing thing'?
A) Because it contains an ambiguous term.
B) Because it is false under both interpretations.
C) Because it contains actual repetition.
D) Because it is not a real property.

Key Takeaway
A good property must be unambiguous; any shared term that allows multiple interpretations invalidates it.

Avoiding Repetition in Defining Properties
Author discusses the fault of repetition in property definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actual repetition (takrār bi-l-fiʿl) and potential repetition (takrār bi-l-quwwa) in property statements and judge whether a property statement is concise.

Core Idea
Repetition, whether explicit or implicit, makes a property statement redundant and thus defective.

The author identifies another fault: repetition (تكرار) in property statements. Repetition can be actual (explicit redundancy) or potential (implicit redundancy). Actual repetition occurs when the predicate explicitly repeats part of the subject, e.g., 'fire is the subtlest body' – 'subtlest body' already implies 'body'. Potential repetition occurs when the predicate implicitly contains the subject, e.g., 'earth is a substance among bodies' – since bodies are substances, 'substance' is already included in 'body'. A good property avoids both types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate in the property statement
What is being defined? What is being said about it? Separate the definiendum (the thing whose property is given) and the definiens (the property statement).
2
Check for actual repetition
Does the predicate explicitly repeat the subject's genus or part? Example: 'fire is the subtlest body' – 'subtlest body' includes 'body', so 'body' is repeated. This is actual repetition.
The author also gives the example 'fire is a body among bodies' as clear actual repetition.
3
Check for potential repetition
Does the predicate implicitly contain the subject? Example: 'earth is a substance among bodies' – since bodies are substances, 'substance' is already implied in 'body'. This is potential repetition.
The author notes that the example is both dialectical and pedagogical, but still redundant.
4
Test a good property
Compare with the author's example: 'man is an animal capable of knowledge' – 'animal' and 'capable of knowledge' do not imply each other, so no repetition.
This is a well-formed property.
5
Judge the property's conciseness
A good property should add new information without redundancy. If any part of the predicate is already necessarily contained in the subject, it is defective.
