The Definiens Must Be More Known Than the Definiendum
Author explains that definitions need more known terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definition violates the condition that the definiens must be more known than the definiendum.

Core Idea
You cannot define something by something that depends on it for meaning.

This passage introduces a crucial rule: any valid definition must use terms that are better known than the term being defined. If a definiens is itself unknown or dependent on the definiendum, the definition fails. This is not just a matter of clarity—it is a logical requirement for a definition to convey understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
In the given definition, find which term is being defined and which term(s) are used to define it.
2
Check if the definiens contains the definiendum or something inferred from it
If the definiens includes the definiendum itself (as in 'animal is that whose species is man', where man is a part of animal), then the definiendum is less known than the definiens? Actually, the author says 'that which is part of animal is known after animal', so the definiens (man) is less known. So we need to assess relative familiarity.
The author gives example: 'animal is that whose species is man' fails because man is known only after animal.
3
Check for opposite or correlative terms
If the definiens is opposite to the definiendum (by contradiction or correlation), then they are mutually dependent and neither is more known.
Examples: day/night, father/son.
4
Check for coordinate species under a genus
Species under the same genus are equally known in terms of genus, so one cannot define the other.
E.g., horse and cow under animal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله إن الحيوان هو الذي نوعه الإنسان لا يكون صحيحًا فإن ما يكون من الحيوان فإنه يعرف بعد الحيوان؛ فكيف يعرف به الحيوان؟ وكذلك إذا أخذ شيئًا ليس أعرف من الشيء لما أنه مقابل له أو هو معه في الوجود؛ وأعني بالمقابل المقابل بالتضاد والتضايف. وأما الملكة والعدم والمتناقضان فالملكة أعرف. والإيجاب كذلك. والأمور التي ليست بمتقابلة بالتضاد وهي معا فهي؛ إما أمور متضايفة، وإما أمور نحو الأنواع التي تحت جنس واحد فلا يتقدم بعضها بعضًا / في المعرفة بوجه، فلا يصح أن يؤخذ بعضها في تعريف البعض. واعلم بأن إعطاء الخاصة وحدها إذا لم يقترن به جنس معلوم لم يكن تمييزا البتة مثلا إذا قلت ضحاك أي ذو استعداد للضحك لم يكن نفس علمك بهذا يوجب أن يكون هذا الشيء حيواناً أو إنسانًا. اللهم إلا أن يكون علم آخر يعلم به فإذن لا بد في الخاصة والرسم من إدخال الجنس. ومما يلي هذه المواضع مواضع لا تتعلق بالإجادة واللا إجادة بل بأنه هل الخاصة في نفسها خاصة أو ليست؛ فمن هذه المواضع أن لا يكون حمل الخاصة صادقًا على واحد البتة أو لا يكون صادقًا على واحد بعينه. فلا يكون ما فرض خاصة بخاصة مثل أن يقول قائل إن خاصة العالم أنه لا يخطئ؛ ثمَّ المهندس قد وجد مع أنه عالم قد يكون مخطئًا فإذن ليست من خواص العالم أن لا يخطئ فهذا المثال صحيح؛ إذ من الباطل أن يكون المهندس يخطئ. فإنه إذا أخطأ فإنه لا يكون مخطئًا من حيث هو مهندس بل باعتبار آخر. وفرق بين قولنا المهندس يخطئ، وقولنا المهندس يخطئ من حيث هو مهندس؛ فإذن
And his saying that animal is that whose species is man is not correct, for that which is part of animal is known after animal; so how is animal known by it? Similarly, if one takes something not more known than the thing, because it is opposite to it or coexisting with it; and by opposite I mean the opposite by contradiction and correlative. As for disposition and privation and contradictories, disposition is more known. And affirmation likewise. And things that are not opposite by contradiction but are together; they are either correlative things, or things like species under a single genus, so one does not precede the other in knowledge in any way; thus it is not permissible to take one in the definition of the other. And know that giving the property alone, if not accompanied by a known genus, is not a distinction at all. For example, if you say 'laughing', i.e., possessing the disposition to laugh, this knowledge alone does not require that this thing be an animal or a human – unless there is another knowledge by which it is known. Therefore, in the property and the description, it is necessary to include the genus. And following these topics are topics that do not pertain to quality and lack thereof, but rather to whether the property is in itself a property or not. Among these topics is that the predication of the property is not true of any individual at all, or is not true of a particular individual. Thus what is assumed to be a property is not a property, such as when someone says that the property of the world is that it does not err; then the engineer is found, while he is worldly [ignorant], may err. So it is not among the properties of the world that it does not err – this example is correct, for it is false that the engineer errs. For if he errs, he does not err insofar as he is an engineer, but by another consideration. And there is a difference between our saying 'the engineer errs' and our saying 'the engineer errs insofar as he is an engineer'.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the conditions for a valid property (خاصة) and description (رسم). The author first addresses the requirement that the definiens must be more known than the definiendum. He criticizes defining animal by 'that whose species is man' because man is part of animal and thus known after animal. Similarly, one cannot define a thing by its opposite (contradiction or correlative) or by something coexisting but not more known. In the case of disposition (ملكة) and privation (عدم), the disposition is more known. Correlatives (متضايفات) and coordinate species under a genus are not prior in knowledge, so they cannot define each other. Next, the author emphasizes that a property alone, without a known genus, does not distinguish. For example, 'laughter' (ضحاك) indicates readiness to laugh but does not specify whether the subject is animal or human; thus genus must be included in both property and description. Finally, the author turns to the veridicality condition: a property must actually hold for the subject. He gives the example of someone claiming that the property of the world (العالم) is that it does not err. But an engineer (المهندس) – who is part of the world (in the sense of being worldly or knowledgeable) – may err. However, the author clarifies that the engineer's error is not qua engineer, but by another aspect. So the property 'does not err' is not true of all instances of the world. The distinction between 'the engineer errs' and 'the engineer errs qua engineer' is crucial for evaluating whether the property holds in itself. This passage thus outlines three key conditions: the definiens must be better known, must include the genus, and must be truly and uniquely predicable of the subject.

رسم
من حيث هو

C) Because animal is more known than man.
D) Because man and animal are opposites.

Key Takeaway
The definiens must be more known than the definiendum; otherwise, the definition provides no epistemic gain.

Property Requires Genus for Effective Distinction
Author explains that property alone does not define without genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a property (khassa) alone, without genus, does not constitute a valid description (rasm).

Core Idea
A property without a genus is like a label without a category; it does not identify what the thing is.

The passage emphasizes that giving only a property—like 'laughing' for humans—does not distinguish the species unless the genus (animal) is also mentioned. Without the genus, the property could apply to many things, failing to uniquely identify. This lesson explores why genus is essential in both property and description.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property given
In the passage, the example is 'laughing' (ضحاك) meaning disposition to laugh.
2
Check what the property refers to without genus
Ask: does 'laughing' alone tell you whether the subject is a human, an angel, or a machine? No. It lacks the categorical framework.
3
Recognize that genus anchors the property to a specific category
By adding 'animal', the property 'laughing' now distinguishes humans within animals.
4
Apply to the definitional distinction between hadd and rasm
Rasm requires both genus and property; hadd requires genus and differentia.

وقوله إن الحيوان هو الذي نوعه الإنسان لا يكون صحيحًا فإن ما يكون من الحيوان فإنه يعرف بعد الحيوان؛ فكيف يعرف به الحيوان؟ وكذلك إذا أخذ شيئًا ليس أعرف من الشيء لما أنه مقابل له أو هو معه في الوجود؛ وأعني بالمقابل المقابل بالتضاد والتضايف. وأما الملكة والعدم والمتناقضان فالملكة أعرف. والإيجاب كذلك. والأمور التي ليست بمتقابلة بالتضاد وهي معا فهي؛ إما أمور متضايفة، وإما أمور نحو الأنواع التي تحت جنس واحد فلا يتقدم بعضها بعضًا / في المعرفة بوجه، فلا يصح أن يؤخذ بعضها في تعريف البعض. واعلم بأن إعطاء الخاصة وحدها إذا لم يقترن به جنس معلوم لم يكن تمييزا البتة مثلا إذا قلت ضحاك أي ذو استعداد للضحك لم يكن نفس علمك بهذا يوجب أن يكون هذا الشيء حيواناً أو إنسانًا. اللهم إلا أن يكون علم آخر يعلم به فإذن لا بد في الخاصة والرسم من إدخال الجنس. ومما يلي هذه المواضع مواضع لا تتعلق بالإجادة واللا إجادة بل بأنه هل الخاصة في نفسها خاصة أو ليست؛ فمن هذه المواضع أن لا يكون حمل الخاصة صادقًا على واحد البتة أو لا يكون صادقًا على واحد بعينه. فلا يكون ما فرض خاصة بخاصة مثل أن يقول قائل إن خاصة العالم أنه لا يخطئ؛ ثمَّ المهندس قد وجد مع أنه عالم قد يكون مخطئًا فإذن ليست من خواص العالم أن لا يخطئ فهذا المثال صحيح؛ إذ من الباطل أن يكون المهندس يخطئ. فإنه إذا أخطأ فإنه لا يكون مخطئًا من حيث هو مهندس بل باعتبار آخر. وفرق بين قولنا المهندس يخطئ، وقولنا المهندس يخطئ من حيث هو مهندس؛ فإذن
And his saying that animal is that whose species is man is not correct, for that which is part of animal is known after animal; so how is animal known by it? Veridicality: A Property Must Actually Hold for the Subject
Author explains that a property must be truly distinctive and veridical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess whether a claimed property is genuinely true of all instances of a subject, using the 'insofar as' distinction.

Core Idea
A property that fails for even one instance is not a property at all.

Even if a property seems distinctive, it must actually be true of every instance of the subject. The passage illustrates with the false claim 'the property of the world is that it does not err'. An engineer, who is worldly, can err. But the author clarifies that error is not due to being an engineer but due to another aspect. This lesson teaches how to evaluate whether a property holds 'insofar as' the subject is what it is.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed property and its subject
In the example, subject: العالم (the world/knowledgeable person), property: لا يخطئ (does not err).
2
Find a counterexample
The engineer (المهندس) is a worldly person who may err.
3
Apply the 'insofar as' test
Is the engineer erring insofar as he is an engineer? No, he errs due to another reason. So the property 'does not err' is true of the engineer only accidentally, not essentially.
4
Determine if the property is truly universal
Since the property fails to hold for all instances (an engineer can err), it is not a genuine property.

Avoiding Repetition in Definitions
Author gives conditions for valid definitions: no repetition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and avoid definitions that contain actual or potential repetition.

Core Idea
A definition must not repeat the definiendum, not even implicitly.

The first condition for a valid definition is that it must be free of repetition. The author illustrates this with examples from the animal and knowable concepts, showing that one cannot be used to define the other because they are independent. He also criticizes a faulty definition that tries to define 'animal' by 'species of man', which is circular. Understanding this condition helps you recognize flawed definitions in Islamic legal and theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the repetition rule
Note the author's statement: 'no repetition at all, neither actually nor potentially'. This means the definition must not contain terms that are synonyms or that imply the definiendum.
Potential repetition occurs when the definition includes something that itself depends on the definiendum.
2
Analyze the animal-knowable example
The author says animal is not included within knowable, and vice versa. So neither is more basic; they are independent. If you tried to define animal as knowable, that would be incorrect because knowable is not prior.
3
Critique the faulty definition
The proposal 'animal is that whose species is man' is rejected because 'species' is known after 'animal'. So the definition would use something less known to define something more known, which is invalid.
This also violates the 'more known' condition, covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للعلم فقد قال شيئًا لا تكرار فيه أصلا لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة؛ إذ الحيوان لا يكون في ضمن القابل للعلم. وكذلك القابل للعلم لا يكون في ضمن الحيوان. وقوله إن الحيوان هو الذي نوعه الإنسان لا يكون صحيحًا فإن ما يكون من الحيوان فإنه يعرف بعد الحيوان؛ فكيف يعرف به الحيوان؟ وكذلك إذا أخذ شيئًا ليس أعرف من الشيء لما أنه مقابل له أو هو معه في الوجود؛ وأعني بالمقابل المقابل بالتضاد والتضايف. وأما الملكة والعدم والمتناقضان فالملكة أعرف. والإيجاب كذلك. والأمور التي ليست بمتقابلة بالتضاد وهي معا فهي؛ إما أمور متضايفة، وإما أمور نحو الأنواع التي تحت جنس واحد فلا يتقدم بعضها بعضًا في المعرفة بوجه، فلا يصح أن يؤخذ بعضها في تعريف البعض. واعلم بأن إعطاء الخاصة وحدها إذا لم يقترن به جنس معلوم لم يكن تمييزا البتة مثلا إذا قلت ضحاك أي ذو استعداد للضحك لم يكن نفس علمك بهذا يوجب أن يكون هذا الشيء حيواناً أو إنسانًا. اللهم إلا أن يكون علم آخر يعلم به فإذن لا بد في الخاصة والرسم من إدخال الجنس.
For knowledge, he said something with no repetition at all, neither actually nor potentially; for animal is not included within that which is knowable. And likewise, the knowable is not included within animal. And his statement 'animal is that whose species is man' is not correct, for that which is from animal is known after animal; so how can animal be known by it? And similarly, if one takes something that is not more known than the thing because it is opposite to it or co-existent with it; and by opposite I mean the opposite by contradiction and correlation. As for possession and privation and contradictories, possession is more known. And affirmation is likewise. And things that are not opposites by contradiction and are together, they are either correlatives or things like species under one genus, so one does not precede the other in knowledge in any way, so it is not valid to take one of them in the definition of the other. And know that giving the property alone, if it is not accompanied by a known genus, it is not a distinction at all; for example if you say 'laughing' meaning 'possessing the disposition to laugh', your knowledge of this alone does not necessitate that this thing is an animal or a human. Unless there is another knowledge by which it is known. So then it is necessary in the property and the description to include the genus.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage examines the conditions for a valid definition (hadd) and description (rasm). The author first addresses the issue of repetition: a definition must not contain terms that are already implied by the definiendum. He gives the example of 'animal' and 'knowable' — neither is contained within the other, so they are independent. He then criticizes a definition that says 'animal is that whose species is man' because 'species' is known after 'animal', so it cannot define 'animal'. Next, he discusses the rule that the terms used in a definition must be more known than the definiendum. Opposites like 'possession' and 'privation' are not equally known; possession is more known. Finally, he emphasizes that a mere property (خاصة) without a genus cannot distinguish the thing defined. For instance, 'laughing' (ضحاك) as a property of man does not by itself indicate whether the thing is an animal or human unless the genus 'animal' is included. Therefore, in both property (خاصة) and description (رسم), the genus must be incorporated.

بالفعل
بالقوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the definition 'animal is that whose species is man'?
A) Because species is not a type of animal
B) Because species is known after animal, so it cannot define animal
C) Because animal is not a species
D) Because the word 'whose' is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be free of any repetition, whether explicit or implicit, and must use terms better known than the definiendum.

Using Better-Known Terms in Definitions
Author discusses the condition that definitions must be using more known terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that definitions must use terms more known than the definiendum, and recognize invalid definitions that use opposites or correlatives.

Core Idea
Define the unknown by the known, not by its opposite or equal.

The author expands on the second condition: the terms used in a definition must be more known (أعرف) than the definiendum. He explains that opposites like possession and privation are not equally known; possession is more known. Also, correlatives and coordinate species cannot define each other. This condition is crucial for avoiding circularity in definitions used in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the better-known condition
The author states that if one takes something that is not more known than the thing, the definition fails. This is because definition is supposed to clarify, not obscure.
2
Examine opposites
Opposites include contradictories (e.g., existence/non-existence) and correlatives (e.g., father/son). Among them, one is more known. For possession and privation, possession is more known. So you cannot define privation as 'absence of possession' without first knowing possession, but possession itself does not need privation to be known.
The author explicitly mentions 'contradiction and correlation' as types of opposites.
3
Handle coordinate species
Species under the same genus (e.g., man and horse under animal) are equally known in that context. None is prior, so one cannot be used to define the other. Definitions must use a higher genus or a more fundamental term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للعلم فقد قال شيئًا لا تكرار فيه أصلا لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة؛ إذ الحيوان لا يكون في ضمن القابل للعلم. وكذلك القابل للعلم لا يكون في ضمن الحيوان. وقوله إن الحيوان هو الذي نوعه الإنسان لا يكون صحيحًا فإن ما يكون من الحيوان فإنه يعرف بعد الحيوان؛ فكيف يعرف به الحيوان؟ وكذلك إذا أخذ شيئًا ليس أعرف من الشيء لما أنه مقابل له أو هو معه في الوجود؛ وأعني بالمقابل المقابل بالتضاد والتضايف. وأما الملكة والعدم والمتناقضان فالملكة أعرف. والإيجاب كذلك. والأمور التي ليست بمتقابلة بالتضاد وهي معا فهي؛ إما أمور متضايفة، وإما أمور نحو الأنواع التي تحت جنس واحد فلا يتقدم بعضها بعضًا في المعرفة بوجه، فلا يصح أن يؤخذ بعضها في تعريف البعض. واعلم بأن إعطاء الخاصة وحدها إذا لم يقترن به جنس معلوم لم يكن تمييزا البتة مثلا إذا قلت ضحاك أي ذو استعداد للضحك لم يكن نفس علمك بهذا يوجب أن يكون هذا الشيء حيواناً أو إنسانًا. اللهم إلا أن يكون علم آخر يعلم به فإذن لا بد في الخاصة والرسم من إدخال الجنس.
For knowledge, he said something with no repetition at all, neither actually nor potentially; for animal is not included within that which is knowable. And likewise, the knowable is not included within animal. And his statement 'animal is that whose species is man' is not correct, for that which is from animal is known after animal; so how can animal be known by it? And similarly, if one takes something that is not more known than the thing because it is opposite to it or co-existent with it; and by opposite I mean the opposite by contradiction and correlation. As for possession and privation and contradictories, possession is more known. And affirmation is likewise. And things that are not opposites by contradiction and are together, they are either correlatives or things like species under one genus, so one does not precede the other in knowledge in any way, so it is not valid to take one of them in the definition of the other. And know that giving the property alone, if it is not accompanied by a known genus, it is not a distinction at all; for example if you say 'laughing' meaning 'possessing the disposition to laugh', your knowledge of this alone does not necessitate that this thing is an animal or a human. Unless there is another knowledge by which it is known. So then it is necessary in the property and the description to include the genus.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage examines the conditions for a valid definition (hadd) and description (rasm). The author first addresses the issue of repetition: a definition must not contain terms that are already implied by the definiendum. He gives the example of 'animal' and 'knowable' — neither is contained within the other, so they are independent. He then criticizes a definition that says 'animal is that whose species is man' because 'species' is known after 'animal', so it cannot define 'animal'. Next, he discusses the rule that the terms used in a definition must be more known than the definiendum. Opposites like 'possession' and 'privation' are not equally known; possession is more known. Finally, he emphasizes that a mere property (خاصة) without a genus cannot distinguish the thing defined. For instance, 'laughing' (ضحاك) as a property of man does not by itself indicate whether the thing is an animal or human unless the genus 'animal' is included. Therefore, in both property (خاصة) and description (رسم), the genus must be incorporated.

المقابل بالتضاد
المقابل بالتضايف
ملكة
عدم

Key Takeaway
Definitions must use terms that are better known than the definiendum; opposites and coordinate species generally cannot define each other.

The Necessity of Genus in Property and Description
Author stresses necessity of genus in property and description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a property alone is insufficient for a description (rasm) and why the genus must be included.

Core Idea
A property without a genus gives only a partial description, not a full distinction.

The author concludes the passage by emphasizing that in both property (خاصة) and description (رسم), the genus (جنس) is necessary. Without it, a property like 'laughing' does not tell you whether the subject is an animal or a human. This is crucial for constructing sound definitions in Islamic sciences, where properties are often used but must be anchored in a known genus.

Guided Path
1
Understand the property/genus relationship
A property (خاصة) is a non-definitional attribute unique to the species, e.g., 'laughing' for humans. But if you only say 'laughing', you don't know if it's a human, a robot, or a spirit. The genus 'animal' provides the category.
The author uses 'laughing' (ضحاك) meaning 'possessing the disposition to laugh'.
2
Analyze the insufficiency of property alone
The author says: 'if you say laughing, your knowledge of this alone does not necessitate that this thing is an animal or a human.' So property alone does not distinguish the species from other things that might share the property? Actually, property is co-extensive, but without genus, the property could belong to multiple categories conceptually.
The point is that property is not self-explanatory; it needs the genus to ground it.
3
Apply to description (rasm)
A description (rasm) is a definition using genus and property. The author states: 'So then it is necessary in the property and the description to include the genus.' Both require the genus for a complete distinction.

Six Conditions for a Valid Property (خاصة)
Author enumerates six cases where a supposed property (خاصة) is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the six conditions that invalidate a claimed property (خاصة) and apply them to evaluate examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A property must be truly co-extensive, non-definitional, not prior, and qualified by nature and primacy.

You already know that a property (خاصة) is a non-definitional attribute co-extensive with its subject. But not every attribute that seems like a property is actually valid. The classical logicians identified six spe of why a claimed property might fail. These conditions are essential for correct definition and description (rasm). In this lesson, you will learn these pitfalls through clear examples from the text, and you will practise spotting them.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of property
A property (خاصة) is a non-definitional attribute that is co-extensive with the subject: it must be true of every instance of the subject and not part of its essence.
2
Identify the six invalid cases from the text
The author lists six situations: (1) property not true of any or one instance, (2) subject made property of its property, (3) differentia taken as property, (4) property exists before subject, (5) property conditional by nature taken absolutely, (6) property applies primarily to one thing and secondarily to another without distinction.
These appear in order in the Arabic passage.
3
Analyse each case: why it fails
For each case, check which condition of a valid property is violated. For example, in case 1, the property 'does not err' fails because it is not true of every scholar (engineers, though scholars, may err qua scholar). In case 2, circularity leads to absurdity. In case 3, the differentia is definitional, not a property. In case 4, the property must accompany the subject at all times. In case 5, a natural property must be explicitly qualified. In case 6, primacy must be specified to avoid sharing with a wider subject.
Refer to the commentary for details.
4
Test a new example
Apply the six conditions to a property claim from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'The property of the prayer (salat) is that it prevents immorality.' Does it hold? Check co-extensiveness, temporality, and definitiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يلي هذه المواضع مواضع لا تتعلق بالإجادة واللا إجادة بل بأنه هل الخاصة في نفسها خاصة أو ليست؛ فمن هذه المواضع أن لا يكون حمل الخاصة صادقًا على واحد البتة أو لا يكون صادقًا على واحد بعينه. فلا يكون ما فرض خاصة بخاصة مثل أن يقول قائل إن خاصة العالم أنه لا يخطئ؛ ثمَّ المهندس قد وجد مع أنه عالم قد يكون مخطئًا فإذن ليست من خواص العالم أن لا يخطئ فهذا المثال صحيح؛ إذ من الباطل أن يكون المهندس يخطئ. فإنه إذا أخطأ فإنه لا يكون مخطئًا من حيث هو مهندس بل باعتبار آخر. وفرق بين قولنا المهندس يخطئ، وقولنا المهندس يخطئ من حيث هو مهندس؛ فإذن ليس يصدق على كل مهندس أنه لا يخطئ إلا أن يقال من حيث إنه مهندس. وقد علمت فيما سلف أن معنى قولنا كل مهندس وما يجري مجراه كيف هو . ومن المواضع ما إذا جعل المخصوص خاصة للخاصة؛ وقد عرف من قبل ما يدل على فساد. وقد قيل في التعليم الأول والموضوع الواحد له خواص كثيرة كل واحد منها غير الآخر»، فلو كان الموضوع خاصة لها لكان خاصة لأمور كثيرة متباينة الحدود فيما كان خاصة : وهذا الكلام في غاية الجودة ) . ومن المواضع ما إذا أخذ الفصل على أنه خاصة، ومنها ما يكون للمبطل؛ وهو أن تكون الخاصة توجد قبل وجود المخصوص لا دائما معها نحو المشي في السوق فإنه لا يمكن أن يجعل خاصة لزيد. ثمَّ من المواضع ما تكون الخاصة فيه خاصة بشرط الطبع فتوخذ مطلقا ؛ وذلك باطل كما إذا قيل إن خاصة الإنسان بالنسبة إلى الفرس أنه ذو رجلين؛ فإن هذا إذا لم يقترن به أن يقال بالطبع لم يكن حقاً؛ إذ ليس كل إنسان ذو رجلين في الوجود. ومن المواضع أن يميز في الخواص ما يكون لشيء أولا ولشيء آخر بعده؛ فإذا لم يميز بعد ذلك لم تكن الخاصة خاصة مثل أن يقال إن خاصة السطح التلون، ولم يقل التلون أولا فإن الجسم يشارك السطح فيه ولا يكون ذلك خاصة السطح. ولو قال ذلك لتحصلت الخاصة بواجبها بل يجب أن تعلم أن المحمولات تختلف بوجوه حملها اختلافا ظاهرا بشرائط تلحقها. فيجب أن يراعى في الخاصة تلك الجهات ليعلم أنها كيف توضع خاصة؛ وقد علمت بأن الخاصة للشيء قد تكون باعتبار الكل، وقد تكون باعتبار الجزء، وقد تكون باعتبار كل واحد منهما كما في الأرض إنها ثقيلة بالطبع ; فنجد الكل وكل جزء بهذه الصفة.
Among the points that follow these are points that do not concern excellence or lack thereof, but rather whether the property in itself is a property or not. Among these points is that the predication of the property is not true of any one at all, or is not true of a specific one. Thus what is assumed to be a property is not a property, like someone saying that the property of the scholar (عالم) is that he does not err; then the engineer (مهندس) is found, even though he is a scholar, he may err. So it is not a property of the scholar that he does not err; this example is correct, for it is false that the engineer errs. For if he errs, he does not err insofar as he is an engineer, but in some other respect. And there is a difference between our saying 'the engineer errs' and 'the engineer errs insofar as he is an engineer'. So it is not true of every engineer that he does not err, unless it is said 'insofar as he is an engineer'. And you have previously known how the meaning of our statement 'every engineer' and its like is. And among the points is when the specific thing (مخصوص) is made a property of the property; and it has been known previously what indicates its corruption. And it has been said in the First Teaching: 'One subject has many properties, each one different from the other'; so if the subject were a property of it, it would be a property of many things with different definitions — and this statement is of utmost excellence. And among the points is when the differentia (فصل) is taken as a property. And among them is what pertains to the invalidator (مبطل): that the property exists before the existence of the specific thing, not always with it, like walking in the market, for it cannot be made a property of Zayd. Then among the points is when the property is a property conditionally by nature, but it is taken absolutely; that is invalid, as when it is said 'the property of man relative to the horse is that he has two legs'; for if this is not accompanied by the statement 'by nature', it is not true, since not every man has two legs in existence. And among the points is that one should distinguish among properties what belongs to something primarily and to another subsequently; if one does not distinguish, then the property is not a property, like saying 'the property of surface is colorability', without saying 'primarily', for body shares in it and it is not a property of surface. If he said that, the property would be obtained properly. Rather, you must know that predicates differ in the modes of their predication with apparent difference due to conditions attached to them. So one must observe those aspects in the property to know how it is posited as a property. And you have known that the property of a thing may be with respect to the whole, or with respect to the part, or with respect to both, as in the earth: it is heavy by nature; we find the whole and every part with this quality.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage lists several cases where a claimed property (خاصة) is invalid. It is a systematic enumeration by the author. First, a property must be true of every instance of the subject and must be co-extensive. An example: 'the scholar does not err' fails because the engineer, though a scholar, may err, but as an engineer he does not err qua engineer. The property must be predicated universally and essentially. Second, one cannot make the specific thing (مخصوص) a property of the property, as that leads to circularity and multiplicity. Third, the differentia (فصل) cannot serve as a property because it is definitional. Fourth, a property must be co-extensive in time, not existing before the subject (like walking for Zayd). Fifth, a property that holds only by nature (بالطبع) must be qualified as such, e.g., 'man has two legs by nature', because not every man actually has two legs. Sixth, some properties belong primarily to one thing and secondarily to another; one must specify 'primarily' to avoid sharing, e.g., colorability is primarily of surface, not of body. Finally, the author notes that properties can be of the whole, part, or both, and gives the example of heaviness of earth.

Key Terms
خاصة
مخصوص
فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is 'not erring' not a valid property of the scholar (عالم) according to the text?
A) Because all scholars err
B) Because the engineer (مهندس) does not err qua engineer, but the property must apply to every scholar, including engineers, and they may err as scholars
C) Because it is not unique to scholars
D) Because it is a definitional attribute

Key Takeaway
A valid property must be co-extensive, non-definitional, temporally co-extensive, and properly qualified by nature and primacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
2. A student says: 'The property of a surface is to be colored.' Evaluate this claim using the condition of primacy. What is the correct formulation to make it a valid property?

Hadd and Rasm

First Two Conditions for a Definition
Author defines the first two conditions for definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first two conditions for evaluating a definition: truth and inclusion of the proximate genus.

Core Idea
A definition must be true and must include the proximate genus.

Every definition in Islamic sciences must meet basic conditions before we even consider its technical merits. The first is truth: the definition must actually apply to what it claims to define. The second is completeness in genus: the definition must give the immediate category (proximate genus), not just any broad category. These two conditions act as a quick filter for any definition you encounter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two spheres of investigation
The author distinguishes between dialectical (general) and scientific (specific) investigation. Definitions are considered more broadly in dialectic, and then specifically in demonstration. The first conditions apply to both.
2
Apply the truth condition (ṣidq)
Check whether the definition is true of the defined thing. If it is not, then it is automatically rejected as a definition. For example, 'human is a rational animal' is true of all humans.
This is the first and most basic filter.
3
Apply the proximate genus condition (jins qarīb)
Check whether the definition includes the immediate genus (jins qarīb) that indicates the common quiddity. If no genus is mentioned, or a wrong genus, or only a remote genus, then the definition is incomplete and fails to convey the essence.
Example: 'human is an animal' uses the proximate genus; 'human is a body' uses a remote genus and is insufficient.
4
Identify common failures
The author lists three failures: (1) no genus at all, (2) a genus that is not the genus of the thing (e.g., 'human is a substance' – substance is too broad), (3) a remote genus instead of the proximate one.
You must know the hierarchical classification (Porphyrian tree) to judge remoteness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: فيما يتعلق بالحدود من الأبحاث الجدلية. قد مر في كتاب البرهان كيفيات اتخاذ الحدود؛ فأما كيفية اعتبارها من حيث هي موجودة فذلك في هذا الكتاب ننظر فيه على البحث الأعم ونستخلص في ضمنه البحث الأخص أعني بالبحث الأعم البحث الجدلي، وبالبحث الأخص البحث العلمي. وقد علمت كيفية كونه أعم من وجه فأول ما يجب أن يراعى في أمر الحد أن ينظر هل هو هو أولا صادق على المحدود؛ فإنه إن لم يكن صادقًا فقد كفى سائر البحث، وعلم أنه ليس بحد. والثاني أن ينظر هل دل فيه على الماهية المشتركة وهو الجنس القريب؛ فإنه وإن لم يكن الجنس مقولا فكان لم يذكر جنس البتة، أو ذكر جنس ليس جنس الشيء البتة، أو ذكر جنس بعيد، وترك الجنس القريب فلم يعلم شيء. وقد علمت جميع ذلك،
Chapter: On what pertains to definitions from dialectical investigations. It has passed in the Book of Demonstration the modalities of taking definitions. As for the modality of considering them from the aspect of their existence, we look into that in this book on the more general investigation and extract within it the more specific investigation: by the more general investigation I mean dialectical investigation, and by the more specific investigation I mean scientific investigation. And you have learned how it is more general in one respect. So the first thing that must be observed in the matter of definition is to look: is it, first of all, true of the defined? For if it is not true, then the rest of the investigation is sufficient, and it is known that it is not a definition. The second is to look: does it indicate the common quiddity, which is the proximate genus? For if the genus is not predicated, then either no genus is mentioned at all, or a genus that is not the genus of the thing at all is mentioned, or a remote genus is mentioned and the proximate genus is left out, so nothing is known. And you have known all that.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new chapter on definitions within dialectical investigations. The author distinguishes between the general (dialectical) and specific (scientific) approaches. The first condition for a definition is that it must be true of the defined thing; if not, it is automatically invalid. The second condition is that it must indicate the common quiddity, i.e., the proximate genus. The author lists three failures: no genus mentioned, wrong genus, or remote genus instead of proximate. These conditions are foundational for evaluating definitions in dialectical debates.

The first two conditions for a valid definition are that it must be true of the defined and must contain the proximate genus.

Correcting the Roles of Body and Soul in Definitions
Author refines how body and soul function in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct placement of body and soul within a logical definition (hadd) for living beings, and distinguish material body from generic body.

Core Idea
In a definition, body is not the genus but a part of the differentia; soul is not a separate differentia.

This passage tackles a common mistake in defining living beings like humans: treating body as the genus and soul as the differentia. The author refines the logical structure, showing that body here is the material body (al-jism al-madi) taken as a component of the differentia, not the generic body (al-jism al-jinsi). Soul is not an independent differentia but part of a composite logical differentia. Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing accurate definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage implicitly refers to living beings (e.g., humans) defined as having a soul. Locate the definiendum in the Arabic: 'that it has a soul' (أنه ذو نفس).
The exact term is not explicitly stated, but the context shows the species being defined is an animated being.
2
Distinguish material body from generic body
The author specifies that body taken as part of the differentia is the material body (al-jism al-madi), not the generic body (al-jism al-jinsi). Generic body is a broader category; material body is a specific type that includes physical composition.
This distinction prevents confusion in the definition's structure.
3
Extract the differentia components
The differentia here includes both material body and soul as components, but not as separate elements. They form a composite logical differentia that distinguishes the species from others.
Soul alone is not the differentia; it is integrated with material body.
4
Test the definition's completeness
The author argues that the composite of body and soul is not the genus; the concept of the composite is not identical to body, but it necessarily implies body. This ensures the definition includes all living beings and excludes non-living things.
Completeness requires that the definition covers all instances and only those instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه ذو نفس؛ ولم يؤخذ الجسم هنا جنسًا بل أخذ الجسم لأنه هو كجزء من الفصل هو الجسم المادي لا الجسم الجنسي، ولم يؤخذ النفس أصلا على أنها فصل في الحد؛ فذلك لا يصح بل على أنها جزء فصل منطقي. وليس أيضًا المركَّب من جسم جنسا لمعنى الجسم فليس مفهوم المركب من جسم هو جسم. وإن كان يلزمه لزوما من خارج فيفهم أن المركب من جسم لا يكون إلا جسما؛ وهذا ظاهر.
That it has a soul; and body is not taken here as a genus, rather body is taken because it is a part of the differentia, which is the material body, not the generic body. And soul is not taken at all as a differentia in the definition; thus that is not valid, but rather as a part of a logical differentia. And also the composite of body is not a genus for the meaning of body, so the concept of the composite of body is not body. And if it implies it by an external implication, then it is understood that the composite of body is nothing but body; and this is clear.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage clarifies the correct roles of body and soul in definitions, particularly for living beings like humans. A common mistake is to treat 'body' as the genus and 'soul' as the differentia. The author corrects this: body here is not the generic body but the material body taken as part of the differentia. The soul is not a differentia on its own but part of a logical differentia that distinguishes the species. The composite of body and soul does not yield 'body' as a genus; rather, the concept of the composite is not identical to body, though it necessarily implies body. This is a subtle point about the structure of definitions in Islamic logic, relevant for defining animated beings.

Intensity in Essences and Properties
Author explains that subjects not admitting intensity do not require intensity in properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a property must intensify with its subject based on whether the subject admits intensity.

Core Idea
Not every property intensifies with its subject; only if the subject itself has degrees.

In defining things, we often associate properties with essences. But this passage warns: do not automatically assume that properties intensify when the essence does not admit degrees. For example, fire moves upward, but moving more upward does not make something 'more fire'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurate definitions in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and its property
In the passage, the subjects are fire and human. Their properties are moving upward and understanding by deliberation, respectively. Write down the pair for each.
These are called 'khassa' — distinctive properties.
2
Check whether the subject admits intensity
The author claims that fire does not admit intensity (ashadd wa ad'af) in its nature, and similarly humanity does not admit intensity. Ask yourself: can something be 'more fire' or 'more human' in essence? Usually not; these are binary essences.
Intensity refers to degrees, like hot-cold or strong-weak.
3
Determine if the property must intensify with the subject
If the subject does not have degrees, then its properties do not necessarily have degrees either. For example, more upward motion does not imply more fire, because fire as an essence does not vary. The author denies the necessity.
The phrase 'laysa yajibu shay' min dhalik' means 'none of that is necessary'.
4
Apply the principle to a new example
Consider the definition of 'ilm (knowledge) in Kalam. Knowledge is a property of the knower. Does the knower (e.g., a human) admit intensity? If not, then more knowledge does not mean the person is more human. But if the knower is Allah, His knowledge is perfect — does that apply? Reflect on how this principle protects definitions from false intensification.
This prevents confusion between essential and accidental degrees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن الموضوع إذا لم يكن قابلا للأشد والأضعف في طباعه فليس يجب شيء من ذلك؛ فإنه ليس إذا كانت النار خاصتها أن تتحرك إلى فوق والإنسان خاصته أن يفهم بالروية يجب أن يكون ما هو أشد حركة إلى فوق أشد نارية، أو ما هو أكثر فهما هو أشد إنسانية؛ وهذا وضع في المقدمة أن الإنسانية تقبل الأشد والأضعف، فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
And know that if the subject does not admit intensity and weakness in its nature, then none of that is necessary; for it is not the case that if fire's property is to move upward and human's property is to understand by deliberation, then what is more moving upward is more fire-like, or what is more understanding is more human-like; and this presupposes that humanity admits intensity and weakness, so consider what you have known, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage warns against mistakenly applying intensity relations to essences that do not admit degrees. For example, fire's property of moving upward does not increase with fire's intensity because fire as such does not have degrees. Similarly, understanding more does not make someone more human unless humanity itself admits intensity. This is a crucial epistemological point for definitions: the properties of a thing do not necessarily intensify with the thing's essence if the essence does not have degrees. The author uses examples from natural philosophy (fire) and psychology (human understanding) to illustrate, grounding the discussion in Kalam and falsafah traditions.

Key Terms

خاصة
موضوع

Key Takeaway
A property intensifies only if its subject admits intensity; do not confuse essential definitions with accidental degrees.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Equality in Generality and Meaning in Definitions
Author explains the difference between equality in generality and equality in meaning for definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between equality in generality and equality in meaning in definitions, and evaluate whether a given definition meets the conditions for a scientific definition.

Core Idea
Convertibility ensures coverage; equality in meaning ensures essence.

In this passage, the author refines the conditions for a proper definition. Having a genus and a differentia is not enough; the definition must be 'equal in generality' (convertible) with the definiendum. However, he warns that even convertibility does not guarantee that the definition conveys the essence. He introduces a finer distinction: equality in meaning is required for a scientific definition, while dialectical definitions can be satisfied with less. Let's break down the key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify 'equality in generality'
Understand that this means the definition and the definiendum have the same extension (apply to exactly the same things). Example: 'human' and 'laughing animal' are equal in generality because all humans laugh and only humans laugh.
This is also called convertibility (in'ikās).
2
Identify 'equality in meaning'
This means the definition expresses the essence (mahiyya) of the definiendum. Example: 'human' as 'rational animal' is equal in meaning because it conveys the essential nature.
3
Compare the two types
The author states that an expression can be equal in generality without being equal in meaning. Convertibility alone does not guarantee that the definition is a proper scientific definition.
For instance, 'human is the laughing animal' is convertible but does not give the essence (since laughter is a property, not a differentia).
4
Test a definition
Given a definition, determine if it is equal in generality, equal in meaning, both, or neither. For example, 'wine is an intoxicating beverage' – is it equal in generality? (Not all intoxicants are wine, but all wine is intoxicating).
Use examples from Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن كان الجنس مذكورًا ولم يكن القول مساويًا لعموم الشيء حتى يكون منعكسا؛ فلم يدل على الحد وإن كان هناك جنس، وكان أضيف إليه ما صار به منعكسا على الشيء. فليس يجب أن يكون الحد جزءا فربَّما كان المضاف إلى الجنس [۳۳۷] خاصة، وربَّما كان فصلًا واحدًا فقط؛ فإنه لا يدل على الماهية أيضًا وإن كان مساويًا، وكذلك إذا أخذت الخاصة فكان الفصل.
ثم القول يمكن أن يكون مساويًا بحسب العموم، ولا يكون مساويًا بحسب المعنى المعتبر هو المساواة بحسب المعنى. وإنه لا يكون مساويًا بحسب المعنى إلا وأن يكون مساويًا بحسب العموم لكن هذا التحقيق لا يراعى في الحدود الجدلية؛ وينفع فيها بما يكون إلحاق الفصل فيه بجعل القول منعكسا على الحدود بل قد ينفع الجدلي بما هو دون هذا
Then if the genus is mentioned and the expression is not equal in generality to the thing so that it is convertible, then it does not indicate the definition even if there is a genus, and something is added to it that makes it convertible to the thing. So it is not necessary that the definition be a part; perhaps what is added to the genus is a property, and perhaps it is only one differentia; for it does not indicate the essence either, even if it is equal. Similarly when a property is taken as the differentia. Then the expression can be equal in terms of generality without being equal in terms of meaning—the considered equality is equality in meaning. And it is not equal in meaning unless it is equal in generality, but this verification is not observed in dialectical definitions; rather, what benefits in them is the inclusion of the differentia by making the expression convertible over the definitions. Indeed, the dialectician may benefit from even less than this.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage refines the conditions for definitions. Even when the genus is present, the definition must be equal in generality (convertible) with the definiendum. However, the author warns that convertibility alone does not guarantee that the definition captures the essence (meaning). For example, 'human is a laughing animal' is convertible (only humans laugh) but does not give the essence. In scientific definitions, equality in meaning is required, but in dialectical contexts, it is enough that the definition is convertible and contains the differentia. The dialectician may even accept less. This shows the flexibility of dialectical argumentation.

Key Terms
مساوي بحسب العموم
مساوي بحسب المعنى
منعكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'human is the laughing animal' not a scientific definition?
A) It is not equal in generality.
B) It is not equal in meaning.
C) It lacks a genus.
D) It is not convertible.

Key Takeaway
Equality in generality (convertibility) is necessary but not sufficient for a scientific definition; equality in meaning is required for the definition to capture the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the definition of 'zakāh' in a typical fiqh text: 'Zakāh is a specified portion of property given to the needy when the property reaches a certain threshold.' Is this definition equal in generality and equal in meaning? Identify any gaps.

Dialectical Definitions and Their Flexibility
Author explains the difference between equality in generality and equality in meaning for definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how dialectical definitions differ from scientific definitions and why convertibility is often sufficient in dialectical contexts.

Core Idea
In dialectical argumentation, a definition need only be convertible to advance the discussion.

The author concludes the passage by noting that the strict verification of equality in meaning is not observed in dialectical definitions. In debate (jadal), it is enough that the definition is convertible and includes the differentia, and sometimes even less is acceptable. This pragmatic approach allows dialecticians to argue productively without requiring absolute precision. Let's explore this flexibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage distinguishes between scientific (ḥaqīqī) and dialectical (jadalī) definitions. Recognize that the standards differ.
2
Identify the dialectical condition
The author says that in dialectical definitions, the requirement of equality in meaning is dropped; only convertibility and inclusion of the differentia matter.
3
Recognize the benefit
This flexibility allows debaters to use commonly accepted definitions or descriptions even if they are not scientifically precise, as long as they are mutually convertible with the definiendum.
Example: In a debate about prayer, a dialectical definition like 'prayer is the act that distinguishes a Muslim from a disbeliever' might be used.
4
Apply the concept
Consider a dialectical exchange about whether a certain act is prayer. The dialectical definition simplifies the argument.
5
Reflect on the limits
The author notes that even less than a convertible definition may benefit the dialectician, indicating a spectrum of strictness.
This shows that dialectical argumentation is pragmatic and goal-oriented.

Pitfalls in Definition Wording
Author lists pitfalls in wording definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify five common pitfalls in definition wording and evaluate whether a definition is well-crafted.

Core Idea
A definition can be rejected if it uses obscure, misplaced, or metaphorical language.

In dialectical debate, not every definition is accepted. Even if the genus and differentia are conventionally appropriate, the wording must be clear and precise. The author lists several flaws that render a definition subject to rejection (man'). Understanding these pitfalls helps you craft robust definitions and spot weak ones in argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five pitfalls
The passage lists: obscure wording, including irrelevant elements, misplacing genus/differentia, using metaphorical/figurative/ambiguous/unfamiliar words.
2
Check each pitfall in a sample definition
Take a proposed definition from fiqh or kalam and test if it falls into any of these traps.
3
Determine if the definition is well-crafted
Based on the list, decide if the definition is acceptable in a dialectical context.
4
Explain why a pitfall makes the definition rejectable
For each pitfall, articulate the logical reason it undermines the definition's purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه إن لم يكن الجنس ولا الفصل جنسًا حقيقيًا بل كان الجنس جنسا بحسب المشهور، والفصل فصلا كذلك كان الحد حدا عند الجدلي. فيجب أن تكون الصيغة الحاصلة من إيراد الجنس والفصل صنعة جيدة فإنها إذا لم تكن جيدة كان في حيز المنع. والوجوه التي بها يكون الحد غير جيد الصيغة هي مثل إغلاق اللفظ، وإدخال ما لا ينبغي، وتحريف ما ينبغي نحو الجنس والفصل عن جهة ما ينبغي، واستعمال اللفظ المستعار والمجاز والمشترك والغريب الوحشي مثلا؛ فإنه إذا كان كذلك فلا يكون الحد على ما ينبغي. وأما أنه كيف يكون حتى يكون حدا علميًا، أو حدا جدليًّا فقد مرَّ من قبل ما يعين على ذلك. وأما أن حد الضد هل يلوح من حد الضد فإنه إذا أعطي شيء ذو ضد حدا ثم لم يظهر منه حد الضد الآخر؛ فليس ذلك الحد؛ إذ حد الضد ضد الحد. وقد يمكن أن يجعل
For if neither the genus nor the differentia is a real genus and differentia, but the genus is a genus according to common usage and the differentia likewise, then the definition is a definition for the dialectician. So the expression resulting from adducing the genus and differentia must be well-crafted; for if it is not well-crafted, it is subject to rejection. The aspects by which a definition is not well-crafted are like: obscurity of wording, including what should not be included, misplacing what should be placed concerning the genus and differentia, and using metaphorical, figurative, ambiguous, or strange uncouth words, for example. For if it is like that, then the definition is not as it should be. As for how it should be to be a scientific definition or a dialectical one, that has already been indicated previously. And as for whether the definition of a contrary is deduced from the definition of the contrary—if a thing that has a contrary is given a definition, then the definition of the other contrary does not appear from it, then that is not the definition; for the definition of the contrary is the contrary of the definition. And it may be possible to make...
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage addresses the practical quality of definitions in dialectical contexts. The author notes that even if the genus and differentia are not real but only conventional (based on common usage), the definition is acceptable for dialecticians. However, the formulation must be well-crafted. Several pitfalls are listed: obscure wording, inclusion of irrelevant elements, misplacement of genus or differentia, and improper use of figurative, ambiguous, or rare words. Finally, a logical test is proposed: if a thing has a contrary, its definition should reveal the definition of that contrary; otherwise, the definition is flawed. For example, if 'heat' is defined correctly, the definition of 'cold' should be logically derivable. This is a powerful tool for checking definitions.

The Contraries Test for Definitions
Author introduces the contraries test for definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the contraries test to check whether a definition is correct in a dialectical setting.

Core Idea
If a thing has a contrary, its definition must reveal the definition of that contrary.

The author presents a powerful test: if a term has an opposite, then a correct definition of one should logically imply the definition of its opposite. This is based on the principle that 'the definition of the contrary is the contrary of the definition'. This test helps detect flawed definitions quickly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its contrary
For the term being defined, determine if it has a logical contrary (e.g., hot/cold, good/evil, necessity/contingency).
2
State the proposed definition
Write the definition given for the term.
3
Derive the definition of the contrary
Based on the proposed definition, logically construct what the definition of the contrary should be (e.g., if 'heat' is defined as 'high molecular energy', then 'cold' should be 'low molecular energy').
4
Check if the derived definition matches the known meaning of the contrary
If it does not align, the original definition is flawed.
5
Apply in a dialectical objection
In a debate, the opponent can use this test to reject a definition that fails the test.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه إن لم يكن الجنس ولا الفصل جنسًا حقيقيًا بل كان الجنس جنسا بحسب المشهور، والفصل فصلا كذلك كان الحد حدا عند الجدلي. فيجب أن تكون الصيغة الحاصلة من إيراد الجنس والفصل صنعة جيدة فإنها إذا لم تكن جيدة كان في حيز المنع. والوجوه التي بها يكون الحد غير جيد الصيغة هي مثل إغلاق اللفظ، وإدخال ما لا ينبغي، وتحريف ما ينبغي نحو الجنس والفصل عن جهة ما ينبغي، واستعمال اللفظ المستعار والمجاز والمشترك والغريب الوحشي مثلا؛ فإنه إذا كان كذلك فلا يكون الحد على ما ينبغي. وأما أنه كيف يكون حتى يكون حدا علميًا، أو حدا جدليًّا فقد مرَّ من قبل ما يعين على ذلك. وأما أن حد الضد هل يلوح من حد الضد فإنه إذا أعطي شيء ذو ضد حدا ثم لم يظهر منه حد الضد الآخر؛ فليس ذلك الحد؛ إذ حد الضد ضد الحد. وقد يمكن أن يجعل
For if neither the genus nor the differentia is a real genus and differentia, but the genus is a genus according to common usage and the differentia likewise, then the definition is a definition for the dialectician. So the expression resulting from adducing the genus and differentia must be well-crafted; for if it is not well-crafted, it is subject to rejection. The aspects by which a definition is not well-crafted are like: obscurity of wording, including what should not be included, misplacing what should be placed concerning the genus and differentia, and using metaphorical, figurative, ambiguous, or strange uncouth words, for example. For if it is like that, then the definition is not as it should be. As for how it should be to be a scientific definition or a dialectical one, that has already been indicated previously. And as for whether the definition of a contrary is deduced from the definition of the contrary—if a thing that has a contrary is given a definition, then the definition of the other contrary does not appear from it, then that is not the definition; for the definition of the contrary is the contrary of the definition. And it may be possible to make...

Commentary
This passage addresses the practical quality of definitions in dialectical contexts. The author notes that even if the genus and differentia are not real but only conventional (based on common usage), the definition is acceptable for dialecticians. However, the formulation must be well-crafted. Several pitfalls are listed: obscure wording, inclusion of irrelevant elements, misplacement of genus or differentia, and improper use of figurative, ambiguous, or rare words. Finally, a logical test is proposed: if a thing has a contrary, its definition should reveal the definition of that contrary; otherwise, the definition is flawed. For example, if 'heat' is defined correctly, the definition of 'cold' should be logically derivable. This is a powerful tool for checking definitions.

A correct definition of a term with a contrary must logically yield the definition of that contrary; otherwise, the definition is invalid.

Co-extensiveness in Scientific Definitions
Author explains the condition of co-extensiveness for definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a given definition meets the condition of co-extensiveness (musawa).

Core Idea
A definition must be convertible with its definiendum, or it fails as a true scientific definition.

In this passage, the author reminds us that genus and differentia are the building blocks of definition, but they only work if the resulting expression is co-extensive with the thing defined. This means the definition must apply to everything that falls under the definiendum and nothing else. We will break down this condition and see why it is essential for precise reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of a definition
The passage refers to genus (jins) as indicating the foundation of the quiddity (mahiya), and differentia (fasl) as completing the quiddity. In a proper definition, both are present.
Remember that genus gives the common essence, differentia gives the specific distinction.
2
Understand co-extensiveness (musawa) and convertibility (in'ikas)
The author stresses that the defining expression (qawl) must be co-extensive: it must apply to every instance of the definiendum and only to those instances. This property is called convertibility.
If the definition is too broad or too narrow, it is not convertible and therefore invalid.
3
Check what happens when genus is mentioned without co-extensiveness
The passage says that if the genus is mentioned but the expression is not co-extensive, it does not count as a definition, even if something is added to it.
Simply having genus plus something is not enough; the result must be convertible.
4
Recognize that the added part may be a property or a differentia
The author notes that what is added to achieve convertibility could be a property (khassa) or a differentia, but if it still fails to be co-extensive, it is not a definition.
Even a property that accidentally applies to everything defined is insufficient if it does not capture the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلمت أن الجنس يدل على أصل الماهية المشتركة، وأن الفصل لا يدل على ماهية النوع وإن دخل في حمله ما يدل حتى تتم به الماهية. ثم إن كان الجنس مذكورًا ولم يكن القول مساويًا لعموم الشيء حتى يكون منعكسا؛ فلم يدل على الحد وإن كان هناك جنس، وكان أضيف إليه ما صار به منعكسا على الشيء. فليس يجب أن يكون الحد جزءا فربَّما كان المضاف إلى الجنس خاصة، وربَّما كان فصلًا واحدًا فقط؛ فإنه لا يدل على الماهية أيضًا وإن كان مساويًا، وكذلك إذا أخذت الخاصة فكان الفصل.
And you have known that the genus indicates the foundation (asl) of the shared quiddity (mahiya), and that the differentia does not indicate the quiddity of the species, even though what indicates enters into its predication (haml) until the quiddity is completed by it. Then if the genus is mentioned and the expression (qawl) is not co-extensive (musawi) with the generality of the thing so that it is convertible (mun'akis), then it does not indicate the definition, even if there is a genus, and something was added to it by which it becomes convertible upon the thing. So it is not necessary that the definition be a part; perhaps what is added to the genus is a property (khassa), and perhaps it is only a single differentia; for it does not indicate the quiddity either, even if it is co-extensive. Similarly, if the property is taken and it is the differentia.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for a proper scientific definition (hadd). It begins by reminding the reader of prior knowledge: the genus indicates the common foundation of the quiddity (the 'whatness' shared by multiple species), while the differentia does not itself indicate the quiddity of the species but rather completes it when added to the genus. The key point is that for a definition to be valid, the defining expression (qawl) must be co-extensive (musawi) with the definiendum; that is, it must apply to everything that the definiendum applies to and nothing else. This property is called 'in'ikas' or convertibility. If the genus is mentioned but the defining expression is not co-extensive (for example, if it is too broad or too narrow), then it does not truly function as a definition. The author explains that adding something to the genus may make it convertible, but what is added need not be a part of the definition in the sense of a differentia — it could be a property (khassa) or even a single differentia. However, if the added element is not convertible with the thing, it still fails. Similarly, if one takes a property (a non-essential accident) as if it were a differentia, the same problem arises. The lesson emphasizes that a definition must be both essential (genus + differentia) and convertible, otherwise it is not a true scientific definition.

الفصل
الحد
المساواة
المنعكس

C) 'Wine is an intoxicating beverage' — covers all wine and only wine.
D) 'Prayer is worship consisting of specific actions' — co-extensive.

Key Takeaway
A valid scientific definition must be convertible (mun'akis), meaning the defining expression applies to all and only the instances of the definiendum.

Definition of Opposite Cannot Be Derived from Opposing Definition
Author argues that, in truth, the definition of an opposite cannot be obtained from its opposite's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between scientific and dialectical reasoning regarding definitions of opposites.

Core Idea
You cannot derive a real definition of an opposite from its opposite's definition.

Sometimes it feels natural to define 'cold' as 'absence of heat', but the logician asks: is that a real definition? This passage explores whether the definition of one opposite (al-didd) can be derived from the other opposite's definition. The answer depends on whether you are seeking truth (tahqiq) or engaging in dialectical argument (jadal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical claim
The author asks: can the definition of one opposite be obtained from the definition of its opposite? He states that if a definition does not reveal the opposite's definition, then it is invalid.
The claim is about the logical relationship between definitions of opposites.
2
Understand the condition for a valid definition
If a thing has an opposite, and its definition does not imply the opposite's definition, then that definition is not a true definition. The definition of an opposite is itself opposite to the definition of the other.
This condition is a test for adequacy of a definition when opposites are involved.
3
Distinguish dialectical from scientific contexts
In dialectic (jadal), you might use the fame of one opposite to infer the other's definition for argumentative purposes. But in scientific truth (tahqiq), this is invalid.
The author acknowledges jadal as a legitimate method for persuasion, not for certain knowledge.
4
Recognize verbal topics
Such dialectical moves are classified as 'verbal topics' (mawadi' lafziyya) – arguments based on language, not on the essence of things.
This alerts you to avoid mistaking verbal arguments for real definitions.
5
Apply the refutation
If the opposite's definition could be derived, then every definition would necessarily reveal its opposite, which is false. This refutation (ibṭal) prevents accepting the dialectical move as scientific.
The refutation is a simple counterexample: many definitions do not reveal their opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن حد الضد هل يلوح من حد الضد فإنه إذا أعطي شيء ذو ضد حدا ثم لم يظهر منه حد الضد الآخر؛ فليس ذلك الحد؛ إذ حد الضد ضد الحد. وقد يمكن أن يجعل هذا الموضع في الجدل موضعا يكتسب منه حد فإنه ربما كان أحدهما قد سبق إلى الشهرة وإن لم يسبق إليها يسبق إلى التسليم؛ فينتقل منه إلى الثاني. وأما في التحقيق فقد ظهر عليك من قبل أن حد الضد لا يكتسب عن حد ضده. ولو كان كذلك فيجب أن يلوح منه حد الضد وإنه للإبطال مانع ؛ فهذه المواضع كأنها مواضع لفظية.
And as for whether the definition of an opposite is disclosed from the definition of the opposite: if something having an opposite is given a definition, and then the definition of the other opposite does not appear from it, then that is not the definition; because the definition of the opposite is opposite to the definition. And it may be possible to make this topic a place in dialectic from which a definition is acquired; for perhaps one of them has preceded in fame, even if it has not preceded to it, it precedes to acceptance; so one moves from it to the second. But in truth, it has become clear to you before that the definition of an opposite is not acquired from the definition of its opposite. And if it were so, it would be necessary that the definition of the opposite would be disclosed from it, and that is prevented by a refutation; these topics are, as it were, verbal topics.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific issue: can the definition of an opposite be derived from the definition of its opposite? The author begins by stating a condition: if something that has an opposite is given a definition, and that definition does not reveal the definition of the opposite, then that definition is invalid. This is because the definition of an opposite is itself opposite to the definition of the other opposite — meaning they are mutually exclusive and not inferable from each other. The author then acknowledges that in dialectical contexts (jadal), one might use this as a point to acquire a definition: if one opposite is more famous or more readily accepted, one could move from it to the other. But this is only a dialectical trick, not a scientific truth. In reality (tahqiq), it has been established earlier that the definition of an opposite cannot be acquired from its opposite's definition. If it were possible, then every definition should necessarily reveal its opposite's definition — but that is not the case, and there is a refuting argument (ibṭal) that prevents it. The author concludes that such dialectical moves are merely verbal (lafziyya) and not based on the nature of things.

Key Terms
الضد (al-didd): An opposite — something that is mutually exclusive with another, such as hot and cold, or existence and non-existence. [Note: Stable term; used in logic and kalam.]
الجدل (al-jadal): Dialectic — a method of argumentation based on accepted premises, not certain truths. [Note: In Islamic logic, jadal is one of the five arts (al-sana'i' al-khamsa).]
الإبطال (al-ibṭal): Refutation — an argument showing that a claim is false or impossible. [Note: No significant shift.]
مواضع لفظية (mawadi' lafziyya): Verbal topics — arguments based on language usage rather than essential reality. [Note: This term is used in the context of dialectical fallacies.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The definition is valid, but the opposite is unknown.
B) The definition is invalid.
C) The definition is dialectically acceptable but not scientific.
D) The opposite has no definition.

Key Takeaway
In scientific truth, the definition of an opposite cannot be derived from its opposite's definition; such a move is only possible dialectically and is inherently verbal.
