Two Types of Priority in Definition: Absolute and Relative
Author distinguishes two types of priority in definitions and argues for absolute priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute priority (a'raf fi nafsihi) and relative priority (a'raf 'indana) in definitions, and identify which type is required for a true definition (hadd bi al-haqiqa).

Core Idea
A true definition must use what is more known in itself, not merely what is familiar to us.

In logic, definitions must be composed of elements that are 'prior' to the definiendum. But 'prior' can mean two different things: ontologically fundamental (absolute priority) or simply better known to human experience (relative priority). The present passage explains this distinction and argues that only absolute priority yields a scientific definition that is unique and essential. Without it, definitions become subjective and multiple, as shown by the example of defining 'man' based on different activities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of priority
Find in the Arabic where the author contrasts 'more known in itself' (أعرف في نفسه) with 'more known to us' (أعرف عندنا). These are the two criteria for selecting definiens.
The author also uses 'aqdam bi al-tab' (prior by nature) to refer to absolute priority.
2
Analyze the examples of line
The author gives two definitions of a line: one based on its beginning (point) which is absolutely prior, and one based on its endpoint which is more known to us. Compare them: which one uses absolute priority?
The point is prior in essence (dhat) to the line, making the first definition absolutely prior.
3
Understand the consequence of relative priority
The author says that if we rely only on relative priority, one thing can have many definitions. Look at the example of 'man' defined by different people as 'animal disposed to sailing' or 'animal disposed to farming'. Why does this happen?
Each definer picks what is familiar to them, not what is essential.
4
Determine the condition for a true definition
The passage states that a definition in truth (hadd bi al-haqiqa) requires both absolute and relative priority. Explain why absolute priority is necessary for scientific knowledge.
Absolute priority ensures the definition captures the essence and is convertible (mutarid mun'akis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من المعلوم أن تكون الأمور المأخوذة في الحد يجب أن تكون مقومات الماهية، ويجب أن تكون أقدم من المحدود بالطباع؛ ولا يمكن أن يكون للشيء الواحد إلا حد واحد، ويكون ذلك بل المعرف أي شيء كان أقدم في المعرفة: إما عندنا وإما على الإطلاق وهو الذي يجب في نفسه أعرف؛ ونحن إذا عرفنا الشيء فيما عرفناه بما هو أعرف في نفسه بأن نقول مثلا إن الخط هو الذي مبدأه غير منقسم أو الذي مبدأه نقطة على أنا نأخذها هنا على ما هو المشهور من أن النقطة أقدم بالذات من الخط. وكذلك الخط من السطح، والسطح من الجسم. وربَّما عرفناه بما هو أعرف عندنا لا على الإطلاق كما قد يعرف الخط بأنه الذي طرفه نقطة. ثم إنه إذا كان لهذه الطريقة لم يكن الحد حدا بالحقيقة وإنما يكون حدا بالحقيقة إذا كان التعريف بما هو أعرف عندنا وعلى الإطلاق. ولو كان الأعرف عندنا مبدءًا للحد للتحديد أمكن أن يكون للشيء الواحد حدود كثيرة بحسب الأعرف عند كل حاد، فواحد يحد الإنسان بأنه حيوان مستعد للملاحة، والآخر أنه مستعد للفلاحة؛ وربَّما كان الشيء أعرف
Then it is known that the things taken in the definition must be constituents (muqawwimat) of the quiddity, and they must be prior in nature (atba') to the definiendum; and it is impossible for one thing to have more than one definition, and that is done by the definiens (mu'arraf) whatever is prior in knowledge: either relative to us ('indana) or absolutely (ala al-itlaq), which is what is more known in itself (a'raf fi nafsihi). And we, when we define something, we define it by what is more known in itself, as we say, for example: 'The line is that whose beginning is indivisible' or 'that whose beginning is a point', taking here according to what is well-known that the point is prior in essence (dhat) to the line. Likewise, the line to the surface, and the surface to the body. And perhaps we define it by what is more known to us, not absolutely, just as a line is sometimes defined as 'that whose end is a point'. Then, if it is by this method, the definition is not a definition in truth; rather it is a definition in truth only if the definition is by what is more known both to us and absolutely. And if what is more known to us were taken as a starting point for the definition, it would be possible for one thing to have many definitions according to what is more known to each definer. For one person defines man as 'an animal disposed to sailing' (musta'idd lil-mallaha), and another as 'an animal disposed to farming' (musta'idd lil-filaha). And perhaps the thing is more known...
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage discusses the epistemological conditions for a valid definition. It begins by reiterating that the components of a definition must be constituents (muqawwimat) of the quiddity and must be prior in nature (atba' bi al-tab') — that is, ontologically prior. It then asserts that a single thing can have only one true definition. The definition must be composed of that which is 'prior in knowledge' (aqdam fi al-ma'rifa). The author distinguishes two types of priority: (1) priority 'relative to us' ('indana) — what we are more familiar with, and (2) priority 'absolutely' (ala al-itlaq) — what is more known in itself, i.e., ontologically prior. An example is given: we can define a line as 'that whose beginning is indivisible' or 'that whose beginning is a point'. The point is prior in essence to the line, so this definition uses what is absolutely prior. Alternatively, we might define a line as 'that whose end is a point' — this uses what is more known to us (since we perceive lines first), but it is not absolutely prior. The author clarifies that a true definition (hadd bi al-haqiqa) must use what is more known both relative to us and absolutely. If we only use what is more known to us, the definition becomes relative and multiple definitions for the same thing become possible, as illustrated by the example of defining 'man' differently as 'animal disposed to sailing' or 'animal disposed to farming' depending on one's perspective. This shows that a single thing could have many definitions if the criterion is only what is more known to each person. The passage breaks off, but the argument emphasizes the importance of absolute priority for scientific definition.

الأقدم بالطبع
الأعرف في نفسه
المعرف
الحد بالحقيقة

Key Takeaway
For a definition to be scientifically valid, it must be composed of what is more known in itself, not merely more known to us.

First Condition: Constitutive Terms (Muqawwimat al-Mahiyya)
Author presents first condition: constitutive terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a proposed definition uses terms that are constitutive of the essence.

Core Idea
A true definition must be built from the very components that make up the thing's quiddity.

The first condition for a valid definition (hadd) is that the terms used must be constitutive of the quiddity (muqawwimat al-mahiyya). In other words, they must express the genus and differentia that compose the essence. This lesson unpacks what it means for terms to be 'constitutive' and how to verify this condition using the example of the line.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the passage – here the definiendum is 'al-khatt' (the line).
2
Extract the proposed definiens
The passage gives two definitions for the line: 'that whose beginning is indivisible' and 'that whose beginning is a point'. Identify the key components: 'beginning', 'indivisible', 'point'.
3
Check if the definiens are constitutive
Determine whether these terms are parts of the essence of 'line'. The author asserts that 'point' is absolutely prior and constitutive because the line is conceptually composed of points. Similarly, 'indivisible beginning' expresses that the line starts from a point.
Muqawwim means something that enters into the essence itself, not an accidental property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من المعلوم أن تكون الأمور المأخوذة في الحد يجب أن تكون مقومات الماهية، ويجب أن تكون أقدم من المحدود بالطباع؛ ولا يمكن أن يكون للشيء الواحد إلا حد واحد، ويكون ذلك بل المعرف أي شيء كان أقدم في المعرفة: إما عندنا وإما على الإطلاق وهو الذي يجب في نفسه أعرف؛ ونحن إذا عرفنا الشيء فيما عرفناه بما هو أعرف في نفسه بأن نقول مثلا إن الخط هو الذي مبدأه غير منقسم أو الذي مبدأه نقطة على أنا نأخذها هنا على ما هو المشهور من أن النقطة أقدم بالذات من الخط. وكذلك الخط من السطح، والسطح من الجسم. وربَّما عرفناه بما هو أعرف عندنا لا على الإطلاق كما قد يعرف الخط بأنه الذي طرفه نقطة.
Then it is known that the matters taken in the definition must be constitutive of the quiddity, and must be prior to the defined by nature; and it is impossible for one thing to have more than one definition, and that is by the definiens, whatever is prior in knowledge: either to us or absolutely, and that is what is more known in itself; and when we know a thing by what is more known in itself, such as when we say that the line is that whose beginning is indivisible, or that its beginning is a point, taking it here according to the well-known [view] that the point is essentially prior to the line. And likewise the line [is prior] to the surface, and the surface to the body. And perhaps we know it by what is more known to us, not absolutely, as the line is known as that whose endpoint is a point.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage sets forth the essential conditions for a valid definition (hadd). First, the terms in the definition must be constitutive (muqawwimat) of the quiddity (mahiyya)—they must express the essence. Second, they must be prior by nature to the defined (aqdam bi'l-taba'). Third, a thing can have only one definition. Fourth, the definiens must be more known (a'raf) either to us or absolutely. The absolutely more known is what is better known in itself, like the point being prior to the line. The line can be defined as 'that whose beginning is indivisible' (using the point as a more known term) or as 'that whose origin is a point.' The example of line relative to surface and body illustrates priority. However, sometimes we define by what is more known only to us, like defining the line as 'that whose endpoint is a point'—this is less rigorous because 'endpoint' is not absolutely more known than 'line' itself.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must use only terms that are constitutive of the essence of the definiendum.

Second Condition: Priority by Nature (Aqdam bi'l-Taba')
Author presents second condition: priority by nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess whether the definiens is prior by nature (aqdam bi'l-taba') to the definiendum.

Core Idea
The definiens must be more fundamental in the order of reality, not just in our thinking.

The second condition for a valid definition is that the definiens must be 'prior by nature' (aqdam bi'l-taba') to the definiendum. This means the terms used must be conceptually more fundamental, not temporally or accidentally prior. This lesson uses the example of the point being prior to the line to clarify this condition.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'priority by nature'
Priority by nature means that the definiens is more basic in the conceptual hierarchy. For instance, the point is prior to the line because a line cannot be conceived without points.
This is not temporal priority but ontological/conceptual priority.
2
Identify the definiendum and definiens
In the passage, the line is defined by the point. The point is prior to the line by nature.
3
Test the priority relationship
If you cannot understand the definiendum without first understanding the definiens, then the definiens is prior by nature. Can you understand 'line' without 'point'? The author says no, because the line's beginning is a point.

Third Condition: Uniqueness of Definition
Author presents third condition: uniqueness of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue why each thing must have only one definition that captures its essence.

Core Idea
One essence, one definition – multiplicity would indicate confusion between essence and description.

The third condition from the passage is that 'it is impossible for one thing to have more than one definition'. This does not mean there cannot be multiple verbal descriptions, but there can be only one definition that truly expresses the essence (hadd haqiqi). This lesson explores the rationale behind this condition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim
The passage asserts that one thing cannot have more than one definition. This is because the essence (mahiyya) is one, so its real definition must be unique.
2
Distinguish definition from description
A definition (hadd) expresses the essence, while a description (rasm) uses accidental properties. Multiple descriptions are possible, but only one essential definition.
3
Apply to the example
The line can be described in multiple ways (e.g., 'that whose beginning is indivisible', 'that whose endpoint is a point'), but its essential definition – what it truly is – is unique.

Fourth Condition: Being More Known (A'raf) – Absolute vs Relative
Author presents fourth condition: more known (absolute vs relative).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definitions that use terms more known absolutely ('a'raf bi'l-dhat) versus more known to us ('a'raf 'indana), and assess which is valid for a hadd.

Core Idea
The best definition uses what is more known in itself, not just what happens to be familiar.

The final condition discussed is that the definiens must be 'more known' (a'raf) than the definiendum. The author distinguishes two types: absolute (more known in itself) and relative to us (more known in our minds). A true definition should ideally use the absolute sense. This lesson examines both types using the example of the line.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two senses of 'more known'
The passage defines 'more known absolutely' as what is more known in itself (أعرف في نفسه), and 'more known to us' as what is more familiar to a particular learner.
2
Analyze the example of the line
Defining the line as 'that whose beginning is a point' uses the point, which is absolutely more known. Defining it as 'that whose endpoint is a point' uses 'endpoint', which is more known to us but not absolutely.
3
Determine which type yields a true hadd
A true definition should use terms that are absolutely more known, because they reflect the objective order of knowledge. Relative definitions are acceptable for beginners but not as rigorous hadd.

The Principle: Relative Knowledge Cannot Ground True Definition
Author contrasts true definition with relative knowledge definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definitions based on relative knowledge ('indana) and absolute knowledge (mutlaq) by analyzing the example of man defined by different dispositions.

Core Idea
A true definition must use terms more known absolutely, not just more known to the definer.

In the previous lesson, we saw that definition requires the definiens to be more known than the definiendum. But what does 'more known' mean? The author now warns: if 'more known' is only relative to a particular person or group, the definition becomes subjective and multiple true definitions could arise. This lesson examines that principle and its consequence through the example of man being defined by navigation or agriculture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for a true definition
Find the author's statement on what makes a definition 'hadd bi'l-haqiqa'. Note that it requires the definiens to be more known both to us and absolutely.
2
Contrast with definitions based on relative 'more known'
Understand the consequence: if we take 'what is more known to us' (relative) as a starting point, multiple definitions become possible for the same thing.
3
Apply the condition to the example of man
Identify the two definitions of man given. Are they using absolute or relative 'more known'? Why are they not true definitions?
4
Reflect on temporal change
The author mentions that what is more known can change with age. How does this further undermine definitions based on relative knowledge?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه إذا كان لهذه الطريقة لم يكن الحد حدا بالحقيقة وإنما يكون حدا بالحقيقة إذا كان التعريف بما هو أعرف عندنا وعلى الإطلاق. ولو كان الأعرف عندنا مبدءًا للحد للتحديد أمكن أن يكون للشيء الواحد حدود كثيرة بحسب الأعرف عند كل حاد، فواحد يحد الإنسان بأنه حيوان مستعد للملاحة، والآخر أنه مستعد للفلاحة؛ وربَّما كان الشيء أعرف في بين الشباب يُصيره غيره أعرف في سن الكهولة. ومما يناسب هذا هو مثل من يعرف الصحة بأنها مقابلة المرض والصحة متجددة، والمرض في التغير وعدمي الذات. وكذلك من يعرف البصر أنه عدم العمى، والعمى عدم ليس له ذات محصلة.
Then if it is by this method, the definition is not a definition in reality; rather it is a definition in reality only if the definition is by what is more known to us and absolutely. And if what is more known to us were taken as a starting point for the definition, it would be possible for one thing to have many definitions according to what is more known to each definer. So one defines man as an animal disposed to navigation, another as disposed to agriculture; and perhaps the thing is more known in youth, then becomes something else more known in old age. And what resembles this is the example of one who defines health as the opposite of disease, while health is continuous and disease is in change and privative of essence. And likewise one who defines sight as the absence of blindness, while blindness is a privation that has no positive essence.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage explains the consequence of defining by what is more known only to us: it does not produce a true definition (hadd bi'l-haqiqa). A true definition must be by what is more known both to us and absolutely. If we rely solely on what is more known to us, multiple definitions arise because different people find different things more known. For example, one person defines man as 'an animal disposed to navigation,' another as 'animal disposed to agriculture'; these are not essential definitions because the dispositions are accidents, not constitutive. Also, what is more known can change with age. The passage gives further examples: defining health as the opposite of disease uses 'disease,' which is privative and less known; defining sight as absence of blindness uses 'blindness,' which is a pure privation with no positive essence. Such definitions fail to capture the positive nature of the defined thing.

Key Terms
حد بالحقيقة
أعرف عندنا
عدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for a definition to be a true definition (hadd bi'l-haqiqa)?
A) It must use terms that are more known to the definer.
B) It must use terms that are more known absolutely and to us.
C) It must use terms that are more known to the general public.
D) It must use terms that are more known to the scholars.

Key Takeaway
A true definition requires the definiens to be more known absolutely, not just relatively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Privative Definitions: Why Health and Sight Cannot Be Defined by Their Opposites
Author criticizes privative definitions using health and sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify privative ('adami) definitions and explain why they fail the test of absolute more known by analyzing the examples of health and sight.

Core Idea
Defining a positive reality by its privative opposite uses something less known, not more known.

The author extends the critique to definitions that use opposites: health defined as opposite of disease, and sight as absence of blindness. Since disease and blindness are privations (lack of health, lack of sight), they are not more known absolutely. In fact, they are less known because they depend on the positive concepts. This lesson unpacks the notion of privative terms and why they cannot serve as proper definientia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions being criticized
Find the two definitions given as examples: health defined as opposite of disease, and sight defined as absence of blindness.
2
Understand the structure of privative definitions
How does each definition use its opposite? Why is this problematic? The definiens (disease, blindness) are privatives that depend on the definiendum (health, sight).
3
Analyze the concept of 'adami
What does the author say about disease and blindness? Note the terms 'عدمي الذات' and 'عدم ليس له ذات محصلة'. Are they properly known?
4
Apply the condition of absolute more known
Why does defining health by disease fail the condition? Why does defining sight by blindness fail? Because the privative term is less known than the positive.

Source Text

For example, one person defines man as 'an animal disposed to navigation,' another as 'animal disposed to agriculture'; these are not essential definitions because the dispositions are accidents, not constitutive. Also, what is more known can change with age. The passage gives further examples: defining health as the opposite of disease uses 'disease,' which is privative and less known; defining sight as absence of blindness uses 'blindness,' which is a pure privation with no positive essence. Such definitions fail to capture the positive nature of the defined thing.

Key Takeaway
Privative terms cannot be used as definientia because they are less known than the positive terms they define.

Six Fallacies Where the Definiens Is Not More Known
Author lists six types of definitions where the definiens is not more known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the six types of definitions where the definiens is not more known than the definiendum, and apply the rule to detect flawed definitions in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A definition fails if its definiens is no clearer than what it defines.

In defining terms, it is essential that the definiens (the defining expression) be better known than the definiendum (the term being defined). Otherwise, the definition does not achieve its purpose of clarifying meaning. The author enumerates six specific positions where this condition is violated, from obvious cases of equal ignorance to subtle circularities and mutual dependencies. Mastering these fallacies sharpens your ability to evaluate definitions in any discipline, especially in the Islamic sciences where precise definitions underpin legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the six fallacious positions
Read the passage and identify each of the six distinct ways a definition can fail because the definiens is not more known. Notice that they range from obvious to subtle.
The author explicitly lists them with examples.
2
Examine the first three: equal ignorance, privation, relatives
In the first example (al-sadiq), both terms are equally unknown. For malaka and 'adam, the positive term must be defined by itself, not by its privation, but the privation is necessarily defined by the positive. For relatives (mutadayifa), each must be defined in relation to the other, but that is acceptable as long as the relation is clear.
Consider why these are problematic.
3
Examine the last three: implicit circularity, mutual definition, species-inclusion
The sun-day example is circular because day is defined using sun. The odd-even example defines each by the other. The even example uses its own species (two) as a part. Each violates the 'more known' condition.
These are more subtle violations.
4
Apply the principle to a new example
Given a definition from Islamic sciences, check whether the definiens is more known than the definiendum. Use the six fallacies as a checklist.
Practice with real examples from usul al-fiqh or kalam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And positions where something not more known is taken include: one is when the taken [term] is equal to the defined and described in ignorance, like 'the true' (al-sadiq), for neither is more known than the other. As for disposition (malaka) and privation ('adam) and contradictory things, the positive among them is defined by itself, for it is distinguished by itself because it is intelligible by itself, by its act, its passion, and its properties. As for the privative, it is only defined by the positive, so it is said that blindness is the absence of sight. As for relative things (mutadayifa), one of them must enter into the definition of the other when their quiddity is predicated in relation to the other; and this is among what has been discussed before. And among them is when a thing is taken in its own definition by way of implication (tadammun) unknowingly, so a thing is defined by what is not more known, as when they say in the definition of the sun that the sun is a star that rises during the day, then day is defined as the time of the sun's movement above the earth; so it is as if he said: the sun is a star that rises at the time of the sun being above the earth. And among them is when things equal in rank under one genus are taken, some in the definition of others, so [the definiens] is not more known, such as when it is said that the odd is that which exceeds the even by one, then it is said that the even is that which exceeds the odd by one. And among them is when the defined itself is taken in its own definition by means of something more specific, such as when they say that the even is that which is divisible into halves, and halves are part of two, and two is a type of even in the apparent view; so its type is taken in its definition. This is in the apparent view. As for in reality, evenness is not a differentia of number nor a genus for its types, but rather it is among the necessary attributes (lawazim) that are non-constitutive of the types of number.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage enumerates six fallacious positions where the definiens is not more known than the definiendum: (1) When the definiens is equally unknown, e.g., defining 'the true' (al-sadiq) by another equally ambiguous term. (2) When dealing with disposition (malaka) and privation ('adam): the positive term (e.g., sight) should be defined by itself, not by its privation; privations are defined by their positives (e.g., blindness as absence of sight). (3) Relative terms (mutadayifa) like father and son: each must be defined in relation to the other, but this is acceptable as long as one enters the definition of the other. (4) Circular definition by implication: defining the sun as 'a star that rises during the day' and then defining day as 'the time the sun is above the earth'—this is implicitly circular. (5) Mutual definition between co-ordinate species under the same genus: defining odd as 'that which exceeds even by one' and even as 'that which exceeds odd by one'—they are equally unknown. (6) Taking the defined itself in its definition via a more specific term: defining even as 'divisible into halves,' where halves are parts of two, and two is a type of even—thus the species (even) is defined by its own type. The author notes that in reality, evenness is not a differentia or genus but a necessary attribute (lazim) of number.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must use a definiens that is better known than the definiendum; otherwise, it fails to clarify meaning.

Definition Errors: Using What Is Not More Known
Author presents two cases of definition error: taking equally unknown and defining privative by positive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two types of definition errors where the definiens is not more known than the definiendum: equal ignorance and defining privation by privation.

Core Idea
A good definition requires the definiens to be more known than the definiendum.

In this passage, the author warns against using definiens that are not more known (a'raf) than the definiendum. Two common faults are examined: taking a term equally unknown (like ‘true’ defined by another equally obscure term) and defining a privative (like ‘blindness’) by a positive (‘sight’) — which, while natural, fails to increase knowledge if the aim is to clarify the privative. Understanding these errors helps us evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fault type
The author distinguishes two ways a definiens can fail to be more known: it is either equally unknown (the definiens and definiendum are both obscure) or it is a privative defined by its positive possession (malaka). Spot these in the examples.
Privative terms (adam) like blindness are naturally less known than their positive counterparts (basar).
2
Analyse the 'equal ignorance' case
The passage gives the example of defining ‘true’ (sadiq) by another term that is equally unknown. Why does this fail? Because you learn nothing new — the definiens is just as obscure, so the definition does not clarify.
This is a violation of the rule that definition should move from the better known to the less known.
3
Examine the privation example
Defining vision as absence of blindness (or health as absence of disease) seems natural, but the passage notes that ‘blindness’ is a non-entity with no essence, so it is not more known than vision. The correct approach is to define the positive (sight) first, then define blindness as its absence.
The muhaqqiqun (verifiers) hold that privatives are only known through their corresponding positives.
4
Apply to a real Islamic definition
Take a definition from hadith terminology, such as ‘sanad’ (chain) — is it more known than ‘isnad’? Or define ‘bid‘a’ (innovation) — does its definiens move from better known to less known? Use the criteria from the passage to test it.
This step helps internalise the rule by applying it to concepts you already know.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في بين الشباب يُصيره غيره أعرف في سن الكهولة. ومما يناسب هذا هو مثل من يعرف الصحة بأنها مقابلة المرض والصحة متجددة، والمرض في التغير وعدمي الذات. وكذلك من يعرف البصر أنه عدم العمى، والعمى عدم ليس له ذات محصلة. ومواضع أخذ ما ليس بأعرف من وجوه؛ منها أن يكون المأخوذ مساويًا للمحدود والمرسوم في الجهالة نحو الصادق فإنه ليس أحدهما أعرف من الآخر. وأما الملكة والعدم والأمور المتناقضة بتحديد الوجودي منها بنفسه مما يتميز بنفسه لأنه معقول بنفسه وبفعله وانفعاله وخواصه. وأما العدمي فإنه إنما يتم تعريفه بالوجودي فيقال إن العمى عدم البصر.
In the youth, something else makes it more known in old age. What is similar to this is like one who knows health as being opposite of disease, and health is renewing, and disease is in change and privative of essence. And similarly, one who knows vision as absence of blindness, and blindness is a non-existent which has no essence. And the places where what is not more known is taken in definition include: that the taken thing be equal to the defined and described in ignorance, like 'true' (sadiq) for neither is more known than the other. As for possession and privation and contradictory things, the existent among them is defined by itself because it is intelligible by itself and by its act, passion, and properties. As for the privative, it is only defined by the existent, so it is said: blindness is absence of sight.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage discusses errors in definition where the definiens is not more known than the definiendum. The author first gives examples: defining health as opposite of disease (where disease is not more known than health), and defining vision as absence of blindness (where blindness is a non-entity). Then the author enumerates the types of taking what is not more known. The first type: taking something that is equal in ignorance to the definiendum, like defining 'true' (sadiq) by something equally unknown. The second type: defining a privative term (like blindness) by the positive (sight). This is acceptable but the privative is less known than the positive, so it is not a proper definition if the aim is to make the privative more known.

Four Faulty Definitions: When the Definiens Is Not More Known
Author enumerates four more types of definition error: relatives, implicit self-reference, reciprocal definitions, and taking species within definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify four types of faulty definitions: relatives, implicit self-reference, reciprocal definitions, and species-in-definition.

Core Idea
A good definition makes the definiendum more known; these four errors violate that by circularity or confusion of levels.

The author continues his critique of faulty definitions. He now presents four specific pitfalls where the definiens fails to be more known. Understanding these helps you evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences, where precise definitions are crucial for legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
For each example, note the definiendum (e.g., sun, odd, even) and the definiens given. Check if the definiens is genuinely more known or clearer.
2
Classify the error type
Determine which of the four errors is committed: relatives (mutadāyifa), implicit self-reference (tadammun), reciprocal definitions (musāwiya fī al-tartīb), or species-in-definition (taking a species as genus).
Relatives: each defined by the other. Implicit self-reference: definiendum reappears in the definiens through a chain. Reciprocal: coordinate species define each other. Species-in-definition: a species is used in the definition of its genus.
3
Explain why it fails to be more known
For each error, articulate how the definiens is not more known than the definiendum. For example, in reciprocal definitions, neither odd nor even is better known because each depends on the other.
The core criterion: the definiens must be better known (a'raf) than the definiendum.
4
Propose a corrected definition
Try to reformulate one of the faulty definitions to avoid the error. For instance, define 'odd' using the genus 'number' and a differentia that does not reference 'even'.
Even a plausible correction may require extra knowledge. The point is to recognize the flaw.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for relative things, one of them must enter into the definition of the other if its quiddity is said in relation to the other; this is among what has been discussed. Among them is when the thing is taken in its own definition by way of implication without one realizing, so the thing is defined by what is not more known, like when they say in the definition of sun: 'The sun is a star that rises during the day', then day is defined as 'the time of the sun's movement above the earth'; as if he said: 'The sun is a star that rises at the time of the sun being above the earth'. Among them is when things that are equal in order under one genus are taken some in the definition of others, so they are defined by what is not more known, as when it is said: 'Odd is that which exceeds even by one', then it is said: 'Even is that which exceeds odd by one'. Among them is when the definiendum itself is taken in its own definition by means of what is more specific than it, like when they say in the definition of even: 'Even is divisible into two halves', and the two halves are part of two, and two is apparently a type of even; so its species is taken in its definition. This is according to the apparent view. But in reality, evenness is not a differentia for number nor a genus for its species; rather it is among the accidental necessary properties that do not constitute the species of number.

Commentary
This passage continues listing types of definition errors where the definiens is not more known. First, relative terms (mutadāyifa) such as father and son must each be defined by the other, which is circular. Second, implicit self-reference: defining the sun as a star that rises during the day, then defining day by the sun's movement — thus the sun is defined by itself. Third, reciprocal definitions of co-ordinate species: defining odd by even and even by odd; each is not more known than the other. Fourth, taking the species (naw') in the definition of the genus: defining 'even' as 'divisible into halves' where 'two' is a species of even. The author notes that in reality, evenness is not a differentia or genus but a necessary accident. These are all faulty because they fail to make the definiendum more known.

Error: Pure Negation as Differentia
Author critiques pure negation as a differentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a differentia is a pure negation and explain why it collapses genus and species.

Core Idea
Pure negation as a differentia makes the genus identical to the species.

When defining, we must avoid using mere absence as a distinguishing feature. The author presents the example of defining 'line' as 'length without width'. He shows that if the differentia is simply a negation, then the genus 'length' itself, considered in its nature, would also lack width, making the species indistinguishable from the genus. This is a critical error in logical definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
The definition is: 'Line is length without width.' The genus is 'length', the differentia is 'without width'.
2
Analyze the differentia
Note that 'without width' is a pure negation; it only tells what the thing is not, not what it is.
3
Test the genus
Consider the genus 'length' alone. The author argues that if we examine the nature of length, width is actually necessary for any length. Therefore, the genus itself cannot be without width. Thus, the definition implies that the genus and species are identical.
The exception is if 'without width' means opposition, not negation.
4
Conclude the error
A differentia that is a pure negation cannot properly distinguish the species; it leads to identity of genus and species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من المواضع ما يكون في اعتبار الفصول وهو أن ما يؤخذ على أنه فصل ليس يدل إلا على السلب المجرد مثل قولهم إن الخط طول بلا عرض؛ وذلك لأن هذا يوجب أن يكون الجنس مشاركًا لنوعه فإن الجنس كالطول إذا نظر إلى طبيعة لم تكن له عرض فإنه إذا نظر في طبيعة الطول، وكان له في حد طبيعة العرض كان العرض داخلا في طبيعة الطول ولازما له؛ فكان لا يكون طول إلا وله عرض فاستحال أن يكون طول ما بلا عرض، فإذن طبيعة الجنس مجردة أيضًا طول بلا عرض فيكون أيضًا طبيعة الجنس وطبيعة النوع الذي هو / الخط واحدة. اللهم إلا أن يعنى بقوله بلا عرض أمرًا مقابلا [۳۳۸] للعرض لا سلبًا؛ وذلك كلام آخر. ومن هذه المواضع أيضًا ما إذا أخذ النوع مكان الفصل كما يقال في حد التعذير إنه شتم مع استحقاق كان الاستحقاق نوعا من الشتم لا فصلاً. وكثيرًا ما يكون فصل النوع كنوع لفصل الجنس. وكذلك من هذه المواضع ما إذا أخذ الجنس مكان الفصل كما يقال إن الفضيلة ملكة محمودة، والمحمودة جنس للفضيلة لا فصل؛ وهذا بحسب المشهور. وأما الملكة والمحمودة فقد يؤخذ أحدهما دون صاحبه فلا يمكن أن يكون أحدهما فصلا للآخر، ولا جنسا كذلك. وقد قيل فيه إنه لا يمكن أن تكون الملكة فصلا والمحمود جنسًا لكن يمكن أن يكون على العكس؛ والكلام فيه يعرف من بعد ومن جملة ما يكون النظر فيه من اللوازم أن ينظر كي لا يكون الفصل محمولا على الجنس، ولا أن يكون الجنس محمولاً على الفصل، ولا أن يكون الفصل من العرضيات؛ فإن فيها من الفساد سواء كان الفصل منطقياً أو غير منطقي. ثمَّ النظر في حقائق الفصول والأجناس وغيرهما من الخمسة يوضح الفساد في هذه الصور؛ وحقائق هذه الأمور مما عرفته من قبل.
Then among the topics concerning differentiae: that what is taken as a differentia indicates only pure negation, like their saying: 'Line is a length without width'. This is because this requires that the genus share with its species: for the genus like length, if one considers its nature, it does not have width for it; but if one examines the nature of length, and width enters into the definition of its nature, then width would be inherent in the nature of length and necessary for it; so there would be no length except that it has width, and thus it is impossible for there to be a length without width. So then the nature of the genus is also abstract: length without width, so the nature of the genus and the nature of the species (i.e., line) would be one. Unless by 'without width' he means something opposed to width, not a negation; that is another matter. Also among these topics is when the species is taken in place of the differentia, as is said in the definition of ta'dhir (insult with merit): it is 'insult with deserving', where 'deserving' is a species of insult, not a differentia. And often the differentia of a species is like a species of the differentia of the genus. Similarly, among these topics is when the genus is taken in place of the differentia, as is said: 'Virtue is a praiseworthy disposition', where 'praiseworthy' is a genus of virtue, not a differentia. This is according to the common view. As for 'disposition' and 'praiseworthy', sometimes one is taken without the other, so neither can be a differentia for the other, nor can they be a genus. It has been said that it is not possible for 'disposition' to be a differentia and 'praiseworthy' to be a genus, but the opposite is possible; the discussion of this will be known later. Among the necessary considerations is that one must ensure that the differentia is not predicated of the genus, nor the genus predicated of the differentia, nor the differentia be among the accidents; for in that there is corruption, whether the differentia is logical or non-logical. Then the examination of the realities of differentiae and genera and the other (five universals) clarifies the corruption in these forms; and the realities of these matters have been known before.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage focuses on errors in selecting differentiae (fusūl). The first error is when a differentia is a mere negation, like defining 'line' as 'length without width'. The author argues that this makes the genus (length) also without width, so the species (line) would be identical to the genus. The exception is if 'without width' means opposition, not negation. The second error is taking the species as the differentia, as in defining 'ta'dhir' (insult with merit) where 'deserving' is a species of insult, not a differentia. The third error is taking the genus as the differentia, like defining 'virtue' as 'praiseworthy disposition' where 'praiseworthy' is a genus. The author then discusses whether 'disposition' and 'praiseworthy' can be a differentia or genus for each other. Finally, the author warns that the differentia must not be predicated of the genus, nor the genus predicated of the differentia, nor the differentia be an accident. These conditions are essential for a sound definition.

Key Terms
فصل
سلب مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is defining 'line' as 'length without width' considered a faulty differentia?
A) It uses a relative term.
B) It reduces the species to the genus, making line identical to length.
C) It is too vague.
D) It uses a species instead of differentia.

Key Takeaway
A differentia must be a positive attribute; a pure negation fails to distinguish the species from the genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition 'Nabidh is an intoxicating drink that is not grape-based' (from Hanafi fiqh). Is the differentia 'not grape-based' a pure negation? Would it collapse the definition? Discuss.

Error: Species Mistaken for Differentia
Author critiques using a species as a differentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect when a species is mistakenly used as a differentia in a definition.

Core Idea
A species cannot serve as a differentia because it is broader than the distinguishing property.

Another common mistake is taking the species itself as the differentia. The author gives the example of defining 'ta'dhir' (insult with merit) as 'insult with deserving'. Here, 'deserving' is actually a species of insult, not a differentia. This confuses the hierarchy: the differentia should be a property that distinguishes the species within the genus, not another species.

Guided Path
1
Examine the definition
The definition is: 'Ta'dhir is insult with deserving.' Identify the genus 'insult' and the supposed differentia 'with deserving'.
2
Analyze the relation
Is 'deserving' a type of insult? Yes, it is a species of insult (a deserving insult). So it is not a differentia but another species.
3
Understand the rule
A differentia must be an attribute that cuts across the genus, not a subcategory.
4
General insight
The author notes that often the differentia of a species behaves like a species of the differentia of the genus, indicating a hierarchy.

Source Text

Why is the definition of ta'dhir as 'insult with deserving' faulty?

Error: Genus as Differentia
Author critiques using a genus as a differentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a genus is mistakenly placed as a differentia in a definition.

Core Idea
Using a genus as a differentia reverses the logical hierarchy and fails to distinguish.

A third error is taking the genus as the differentia. The author gives the example: 'Virtue is a praiseworthy disposition.' Here, 'praiseworthy' is actually a genus of virtue, not a differentia. This confuses the logical order: the differentia should come from within the genus, not be the genus itself.

Guided Path
1
Examine the definition
The definition is: 'Virtue is a praiseworthy disposition.' The supposed differentia is 'praiseworthy', and the genus is 'disposition' (malaka).
2
Analyze the relation
Is 'praiseworthy' a genus of virtue? Yes, virtue is a type of praiseworthy thing. So 'praiseworthy' is a genus, not a differentia.
3
Understand the rule
A differentia must be subordinate to the genus; it cannot be a genus itself.
4
Consider alternative orders
The author notes that sometimes 'disposition' and 'praiseworthy' cannot serve as differentia or genus for each other, but one order might be possible. This shows the complexity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then among the topics concerning differentiae: that what is taken as a differentia indicates only pure negation, like their saying: 'Line is a length without width'. In the faulty definition 'Virtue is a praiseworthy disposition', why is 'praiseworthy' not a valid differentia?

Conditions for a Valid Differentia
Author states conditions for a valid differentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to check whether a differentia violates the conditions: not predicated of the genus, not having the genus predicated of it, and not being an accident.

Core Idea
A valid differentia must not be predicated of its genus, nor have its genus predicated of it, and must be essential, not accidental.

After presenting three specific errors, the author lays down general conditions for any differentia. These conditions ensure that the differentia plays its proper role in distinguishing a species within a genus. They apply to both logical and non-logical differentiae. Understanding these conditions requires knowledge of the five universals.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three conditions
The author mentions three: (1) the differentia must not be predicated of the genus, (2) the genus must not be predicated of the differentia, (3) the differentia must not be an accident.
2
Apply condition 1
Check if the differentia can be true of the genus as a whole. For example, 'rational' is not predicated of 'animal' because not all animals are rational, so condition 1 is satisfied for the definition of 'human'.
3
Apply condition 2
Check if the genus can be predicated of the differentia. For 'human', the differentia 'rational' is not itself a species of 'animal'? Actually, 'rational' is a property, not a species. The genus should not be said of the differentia; if it were, the differentia would be a species.
4
Apply condition 3
Ensure the differentia is not an accidental property. For example, 'walking' is an accident, so it cannot be the differentia for 'human'.
5
Refer to the five universals
Understanding these conditions requires knowing the real definitions of genera, species, differentiae, etc., as discussed earlier.

ثم من المواضع ما يكون في اعتبار الفصول وهو أن ما يؤخذ على أنه فصل ليس يدل إلا على السلب المجرد مثل قولهم إن الخط طول بلا عرض؛ وذلك لأن هذا يوجب أن يكون الجنس مشاركًا لنوعه فإن الجنس كالطول إذا نظر إلى طبيعة لم تكن له عرض فإنه إذا نظر في طبيعة الطول، وكان له في حد طبيعة العرض كان العرض داخلا في طبيعة الطول ولازما له؛ فكان لا يكون طول إلا وله عرض فاستحال أن يكون طول ما بلا عرض، فإذن طبيعة الجنس مجردة أيضًا طول بلا عرض فيكون أيضًا طبيعة الجنس وطبيعة النوع الذي هو / الخط واحدة. اللهم إلا أن يعنى بقوله بلا عرض أمرًا مقابلا [۳۳۸] للعرض لا سلبًا؛ وذلك كلام آخر. ومن هذه المواضع أيضًا ما إذا أخذ النوع مكان الفصل كما يقال في حد التعذير إنه شتم مع استحقاق كان الاستحقاق نوعا من الشتم لا فصلاً. وكثيرًا ما يكون فصل النوع كنوع لفصل الجنس. وكذلك من هذه المواضع ما إذا أخذ الجنس مكان الفصل كما يقال إن الفضيلة ملكة محمودة، والمحمودة جنس للفضيلة لا فصل؛ وهذا بحسب المشهور. وأما الملكة والمحمودة فقد يؤخذ أحدهما دون صاحبه فلا يمكن أن يكون أحدهما فصلا للآخر، ولا جنسا كذلك. وقد قيل فيه إنه لا يمكن أن تكون الملكة فصلا والمحمود جنسًا لكن يمكن أن يكون على العكس؛ والكلام فيه يعرف من بعد ومن جملة ما يكون النظر فيه من اللوازم أن ينظر كي لا يكون الفصل محمولا على الجنس، ولا أن يكون الجنس محمولاً على الفصل، ولا أن يكون الفصل من العرضيات؛ فإن فيها من الفساد سواء كان الفصل منطقياً أو غير منطقي. ثمَّ النظر في حقائق الفصول والأجناس وغيرهما من الخمسة يوضح الفساد في هذه الصور؛ وحقائق هذه الأمور مما عرفته من قبل.
Then among the topics concerning differentiae: that what is taken as a differentia indicates only pure negation, like their saying: 'Line is a length without width'. This is because this requires that the genus share with its species: for the genus like length, if one considers its nature, it does not have width for it; but if one examines the nature of length, and width enters into the definition of its nature, then width would be inherent in the nature of length and necessary for it; so there would be no length except that it has width, and thus it is impossible for there to be a length without width. So then the nature of the genus is also abstract: length without width, so the nature of the genus and the nature of the species (i.e., line) would be one. Unless by 'without width' he means something opposed to width, not a negation; that is another matter. Also among these topics is when the species is taken in place of the differentia, as is said in the definition of ta'dhir (insult with merit): it is 'insult with deserving', where 'deserving' is a species of insult, not a differentia. And often the differentia of a species is like a species of the differentia of the genus. Similarly, among these topics is when the genus is taken in place of the differentia, as is said: 'Virtue is a praiseworthy disposition', where 'praiseworthy' is a genus of virtue, not a differentia. This is according to the common view. As for 'disposition' and 'praiseworthy', sometimes one is taken without the other, so neither can be a differentia for the other, nor can they be a genus. It has been said that it is not possible for 'disposition' to be a differentia and 'praiseworthy' to be a genus, but the opposite is possible; the discussion of this will be known later. Among the necessary considerations is that one must ensure that the differentia is not predicated of the genus, nor the genus predicated of the differentia, nor the differentia be among the accidents; for in that there is corruption, whether the differentia is logical or non-logical. Then the examination of the realities of differentiae and genera and the other (five universals) clarifies the corruption in these forms; and the realities of these matters have been known before.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage focuses on errors in selecting differentiae (fusūl). The first error is when a differentia is a mere negation, like defining 'line' as 'length without width'. The author argues that this makes the genus (length) also without width, so the species (line) would be identical to the genus. The exception is if 'without width' means opposition, not negation. The second error is taking the species as the differentia, as in defining 'ta'dhir' (insult with merit) where 'deserving' is a species of insult, not a differentia. The third error is taking the genus as the differentia, like defining 'virtue' as 'praiseworthy disposition' where 'praiseworthy' is a genus. The author then discusses whether 'disposition' and 'praiseworthy' can be a differentia or genus for each other. Finally, the author warns that the differentia must not be predicated of the genus, nor the genus predicated of the differentia, nor the differentia be an accident. These conditions are essential for a sound definition.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for a valid differentia according to the passage?
A) The differentia must not be predicated of the genus.
B) The genus must not be predicated of the differentia.
C) The differentia must be an accident.
D) The differentia must not be an accident.

Key Takeaway
A valid differentia must not be predicated of its genus, nor have its genus predicated of it, and must be essential, not accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition 'Zakat is a specified charity on wealth'. Check if the differentia 'specified' meets the three conditions. Is it predicated of the genus 'charity'? Is the genus predicated of it? Is it an accident?

The Fallacy of Negation as a Differentia
Author shows why negation cannot serve as a differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a purported differentia is merely a negation and explain why it renders the definition invalid.

Core Idea
A differentia that is merely a negation collapses genus and species.

The author opens the discussion of problematic cases for differentiae with a subtle error: using a pure negation as the distinguishing feature. The example 'length without breadth' seems to define a line, but it inadvertently makes the genus and species identical. Let's unpack why.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Identify the definiendum ('line') and the proposed definition: 'length without breadth'. Note that 'length' is the genus and 'without breadth' is the purported differentia.
2
Analyse the differentia: negation or opposite?
Determine whether 'without breadth' is a pure negation (i.e., simply the absence of breadth) or a positive opposite (like 'breadthless' as a property). Pure negation is insufficient for a differentia.
3
Trace the consequence
If 'without breadth' is pure negation, then the genus 'length' itself (in its abstract nature) also lacks breadth, since breadth is not part of length's essence. Thus the genus and species would share the same nature, making them identical.
4
Recognize the exception
The author notes that if 'without breadth' means 'opposite to breadth' (a positive privation), the error is avoided. Distinguish between negation (salab) and opposition (muqabila).
This nuance is important for later discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then among the topics is what concerns the consideration of differentiae: that which is taken as a differentia but indicates only pure negation, like their saying 'the line is length without breadth.' This is because that would necessitate that the genus shares [in the nature] with its species, for the genus, like length, when considered in its nature, does not have a breadth, and when considered in the nature of length, and if breadth were a part of the nature of breadth, then breadth would be internal to the nature of length and inseparable from it; so there would be no length except that it has breadth, so it is impossible for a length to exist without breadth. Thus, the nature of the genus abstractedly is also 'length without breadth,' so the nature of the genus and the nature of the species, which is the line, become one. Unless by 'without breadth' they mean something opposite to breadth, not negation; that is another discourse. And among these topics also is when a species is taken in place of the differentia, as is said in the definition of 'ta'dhir' (chastisement) that it is 'insult with desert' — desert [here] is a species of insult, not a differentia. And often the differentia of a species is like a species of the differentia of the genus. Likewise among these topics is when the genus is taken in place of the differentia, as is said that virtue is 'a praiseworthy disposition' — 'praiseworthy' is a genus for virtue, not a differentia; this is according to the well-known view. As for 'disposition' and 'praiseworthy,' one may be taken without the other, so it is not possible for one to be a differentia for the other, nor a genus likewise. And it has been said therein that it is not possible for 'disposition' to be a differentia and 'praiseworthy' to be a genus, but it is possible the other way around; the discussion of this is to be known later. And among what must be observed from the requirements is to ensure that the differentia is not predicated of the genus, nor the genus predicated of the differentia, nor that the differentia be among the accidental properties; for in that there is corruption, whether the differentia is logical or non-logical. Then consideration of the realities of differentiae and genera and the rest of the five universals clarifies the corruption in these forms; and the realities of these things you have known before.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of common mistakes made when defining things using differentiae. The author lists several fallacies: first, using a mere negation as a differentia, such as defining a line as 'length without breadth'. The problem is that if 'without breadth' is a pure negation, then the genus 'length' itself would also be 'without breadth' (since 'length' as a genus has no breadth), making the genus and the species 'line' identical. The only way out is if 'without breadth' means opposition to breadth, not mere negation. Second, using a species as a differentia, e.g., defining 'chastisement' as 'insult with desert', where 'desert' is actually a species of insult. Third, using a genus as a differentia, e.g., defining virtue as 'a praiseworthy disposition', where 'praiseworthy' is a genus of virtue. The author also notes that sometimes a differentia of a species may be like a species of the differentia of the genus, implying a hierarchical relation. He then emphasizes general rules: the differentia must not be predicated of the genus, nor the genus of the differentia, and the differentia must not be an accidental property. These are conditions for a sound definition.

D) Because the definition is too long.

Key Takeaway
A differentia that is only a negation makes the definition circular or trivial.

?

Mistaking Species and Genus for Differentiae
Author lists and refutes definitions where species or genus are used as differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid differentia and a species or genus mistakenly used as a differentia.

Core Idea
A differentia must be neither a species nor a genus of the definiendum.

The author continues with two more common mistakes: taking a species as a differentia, and taking a genus as a differentia. These errors violate the logical hierarchy of genus, species, and differentia. Let's examine the examples from the text and the general rules that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'ta'dhir'
Find the definition: 'ta'dhir' (chastisement) is 'insult with desert'. Here 'desert' (istihqaq) is taken as the differentia.
2
Check if 'desert' is a species or differentia
The author states that 'desert' is a species of 'insult' (shatm), not a differentia. A differentia must distinguish the species within the genus, but here 'desert' is a subtype of insult, so it is too narrow and does not function as a differentia.
3
Analyse the definition of 'virtue'
Virtue is defined as 'a praiseworthy disposition' (malaka mahmuda). The author says 'praiseworthy' (mahmud) is a genus of virtue, not a differentia. Virtue is a species under the genus 'praiseworthy quality', so 'praiseworthy' is too broad and does not delimit the species.
4
Apply the general conditions
The author gives three rules: (1) the differentia must not be predicated of the genus, (2) the genus must not be predicated of the differentia, (3) the differentia must not be an accidental property. Violating these leads to corruption.
These rules ensure the differentia is properly subordinate within the definitional structure.

Precision in Relational Definitions
Author gives examples of knowledge, grammar, and the distinction between relative differentia and relative-in-a-special-way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a relational definition uses a proper special relation (إضافة خاصة) or merely a generic attribution.

Core Idea
Defining relational things requires a special relation, not just any relative attribution.

In defining things that are inherently relational, such as knowledge or grammar, we must be careful to express the exact relation that forms their essence. The author illustrates this using two familiar examples from the Islamic sciences and then draws a critical distinction: there is a difference between saying the differentia is merely relative (مضاف) and saying it is relative in a specific way (مضاف إضافة خاصة). Without this precision, the definition fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thing being defined
In the passage, two examples are given: 'knowledge' and 'grammar'. Note that both are defined relationally—knowledge is related to its theoretical and practical parts, and grammar is defined as a species of knowledge.
The definiendum is not always a simple substance; it can be a relational entity.
2
Extract the genus and differentia from the definition
For grammar, the genus is 'knowledge', and the differentia is 'of what occurs to language from a certain aspect'. This differentia expresses a relation to language.
The differentia is clearly relational, not absolute.
3
Assess whether the differentia captures a 'special relation' or just a general one
The author warns that a merely generic relation (like 'related to language') is insufficient; it must be a special relation that uniquely distinguishes grammar from other sciences of language, such as morphology or rhetoric.
Think: Does the definition 'knowledge of what occurs to language' include all of grammar and only grammar?
4
Check if the definition identifies the correct aspect and direction of the relation
The passage warns that some relational things may have two relations—one real, one accidental. If the definition points to the wrong relation or the wrong aspect, it is flawed.
For example, defining 'father' as 'someone who has a child' captures the real relation, but defining 'father' as 'someone who is older than another' would be accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمثال الآخر أن العلم منه نظري، ومنه عملي؛ فالعلم شيء ذو إضافة لذلك النظري والعملي. وأما النحو فهو نوع منه لا فصل فيقال في حده إنه علم لما يعرض للغة من جهة كذا وكذا؛ وفصله إضافي لا محالة، وفرق بين أن يقول إن الفصل مضاف، وبين أن يقول إنه مضاف إضافة خاصة، وموضع آخر أنه قد يكون لبعض المضافات بالمعنى العام إضافتان إلى الجانبين؛ وربَّما كان أحدهما بالحقيقة، والآخر بنوع من العرض فإذا لم تكن الإضافة واقعة إلى الشيء ينبغي أن يكون إليه. ومن الجهة التي تنبغي لم يكن التحديد جيدا.
Another example: Knowledge is partly theoretical and partly practical; so knowledge is something possessing relation to the theoretical and practical. As for grammar, it is a species of it (knowledge) with no differentia, so it is said in its definition: it is knowledge of what occurs to language from a certain aspect; and its differentia is necessarily relational. And there is a difference between saying that the differentia is relative, and saying that it is relative with a special relation. And another place: Some relative things in the general sense may have two relations to two sides; and perhaps one of them is real, and the other is accidental. So if the relation does not occur to the thing to which it ought to be, and from the aspect which it ought to be, the definition is not good.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage provides examples and further clarifications about defining relational things. The author first takes 'knowledge' (العلم) as something that has both theoretical and practical parts; knowledge itself is relational to those parts. Then he mentions grammar (النحو) as a species of knowledge; its definition includes the relational differentia 'knowledge of what occurs to language'. The author then distinguishes between the differentia being merely relative (مضاف) and being relative in a specific way (مضاف إضافة خاصة). This distinction is crucial because the differentia must capture the precise relation. Furthermore, some relative things can have two relations to two different things, one real and the other accidental. If the definition does not correctly identify the proper relation, it is flawed. This shows that defining relational things requires careful attention to the exact nature and direction of the relation.

Key Terms
إضافة خاصة (iḍāfa khāṣṣa): special relation
فصل إضافي (faṣl iḍāfi): relational differentia
مضاف (muḍāf): relative/attributed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The differentia must be a category
B) The differentia must be relative in a special way
C) The differentia must be absolute
D) The differentia must be a genus

Key Takeaway
For a definition to be precise, a relational differentia must indicate a special relation, not just any attribution.

Defining Relational Things: The Case of Sight
Author critiques flawed definitions of sight, understanding, and love, showing proper relational constraints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential from accidental relations in a definition of a relational thing.

Core Idea
A definition of a relational thing must capture what it is in itself, not an accessory relation.

When defining things that have multiple relations, like sight (which relates to both the seer and the seen), it is easy to mistake an accidental relation for the essential one. The author uses sight as a warning: we must define sight by its essential function—being the instrument by which the animal sees—not by its relation to a seer. This lesson teaches you to spot such errors in any definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thing being defined
In the passage, the thing is 'sight' (al-basar). Note that it has two relations: to the seer (al-mubsir) and to the seen (al-mubsar). But these are not equally essential.
2
Find the essential function
The author argues that sight is essentially 'that by which the animal sees colors through vision.' The definition must state what sight does in itself, not an accompanying circumstance.
The relation to the seen is essential; the relation to the seer is necessary but not constitutive.
3
Contrast with a flawed definition
Imagine defining sight as 'the instrument related to the seer.' That would be wrong because it focuses on an accessory relation. The author says such a definition would be like saying a sight exists 'for itself' independent of a seer—absurd.
4
Extract the correct definitional formula
For a relational thing, start with its genus (instrument) and add the specific differentia (by which seeing occurs). The author's formula: 'an instrument by which the animal sees colors through vision.'
This is a hadd (real definition) because it captures the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثلا إذا حد البصر الذي له إضافة إلى المُبصِر وإلى المُبصر لكنه إنما هو بصر لأنه يبصر به شيء ما فلا يمكن يتوهم البصر بصرا إلا وهو الذي يمكن به من تحقق المبصر بالنظر. ولا يبعد أن يتوهم بصرًا مفردًا ليس لشيء آخر فهو بصر لنفسه لا على أنه آلة لغيره فيكون تعلقه بالمبصر أمرًا في هويته، وتعلقه بمبصر هو البتة أمرًا لازمًا؛ فيجب إذن أن يراعى في التحديد فيقال إنه آلة بها يبصر الحيوان الألوان بالنظر . وعلى هذا إذا كان للشيء إضافة ما . وأما قولهم في تحديد الفهم إنه ملكة للنفس استعداد به نحو سرعة إدراك ما يرد عليها فذلك لا يصح لما أن الفهم أو لا ملكة لجزء ما للنفس وهي القوة الفكرية؛ ثمَّ للنفس ثمَّ للإنسان. وكذلك قولهم في العشق إنه شهوة الجماع؛ والعشق إذا.
For example, if you define sight, which has a relation to the seer and to the seen, but it is sight because by it something is seen; so it is not possible to conceive of sight as sight unless it is that by which the actualization of the seen occurs through vision. And it is not far-fetched that one might conceive of a solitary sight not for something else—it is sight for itself, not as an instrument for another. So its attachment to the seen is a matter in its identity, and its attachment to a seer is necessarily a necessary matter. Therefore, one must observe in the definition and say: It is an instrument by which the animal sees colors through vision. And likewise if a thing has some relation. As for their statement defining understanding (al-fahm) as a faculty of the soul with a disposition toward quickly grasping what presents itself—that is not valid, because understanding is first a faculty of a part of the soul, which is the intellectual power; then of the soul; then of the human. And likewise their statement in defining love (al-'ishq) as sexual desire—and love if...
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of definitions of things that have dual relational aspects. The author first addresses sight (البصر), which relates to both the seer (المبصر) and the seen (المبصر) [note: same Arabic spelling but different meanings]. He argues that sight is essentially that by which the animal sees colors; anything else is accidental. The definition must be: 'an instrument by which the animal sees colors through vision.' Then he critiques the definition of understanding (الفهم) as 'a faculty with a disposition for quick comprehension.' The author argues that understanding is primarily a faculty of the intellectual power, then of the soul, then of the person—the definition misses the specific relational nature. Finally, he begins critiquing the definition of love (العشق) as 'sexual desire' (شهوة الجماع), but the text cuts off. The passage emphasizes that definitions must capture the real relation (what the thing is essentially) rather than accidental or non-specific relations.

Key Takeaway
For a relational thing, the definition must capture the essential relation that constitutes its identity, not a merely necessary relation.

The Specificity of Understanding: Why General Dispositions Fail
Author critiques flawed definitions of sight, understanding, and love, showing proper relational constraints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definition is too broad by failing to specify the appropriate genus or part.

Core Idea
Defining a specific power by a general disposition misses the distinct parts of the soul.

The definition of understanding (al-fahm) as 'a faculty of the soul with a disposition for quick comprehension' seems plausible, but the author rejects it. He insists that understanding is not just any faculty of the soul; it belongs specifically to the intellectual power. This lesson teaches you to check whether a definition's genus is precise enough to capture the thing's unique reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thing being defined
The thing is 'understanding' (al-fahm). Some define it as a faculty with a disposition for quick comprehension.
2
Check the genus in the definition
The definition says 'a faculty of the soul.' This is too broad because the soul has multiple faculties: appetitive, irascible, rational, etc. Understanding is not just any faculty.
The author says understanding is first a faculty of the intellectual power (al-quwwa al-fikriyya), then of the soul, then of the human. So the immediate genus should be 'an intellectual faculty.'
3
Analyze the differentia
The differentia 'disposition toward quick grasping' is not specific to understanding; other faculties might have speed. The differentia should capture what understanding does essentially, which is intellectual perception.
4
Formulate a corrected definition
Based on the author's hints, a proper definition would be: 'An intellectual faculty of the soul through which it perceives intelligible meanings.' This specifies the genus (intellectual faculty) and the function (perception of intelligibles).
The text does not give the full corrected definition, but this inference follows his logic.
